Terms:          IBRD:  20 years, including a five-year grace period,
at the standard variable interest rate.
IDA:   Standard, with 35 years' maturity.
Proiect DescriRtion:
The project's prime objectives are:
(a)  to improve the quality of investment planning in highways and
to rationalize the financing, planning, and implementation of
road maintenance/improvement through the application of a
systematic maintenance management program; and
(b)  to assist in rectifying the extensive and long-standing
deficiencies in the condition, structure, and capacity of the
road networks of the selected states by the introduction of
larger coastruction contracts of sufficient individual size
to encourage local firms to mechanize, but which, when
grouped, would attract international interest.
The project comprises:
(a)  the reconstruction and widening to two lanes of about
2,300 km of mostly single-lane roads in the four states;
(b)  the procurement of specialized equipment to assist those
states in monitoring the condition and usage of their
networks, and to promote road safety;
(c)  staff training and technical assistance which would emphasize
the planning, design, and implementation of projects and
developing the skills of road iiaintenance field staff; and
(d)  consultants' services for the introduction of pavement
management systems in each of the four states, and for
supervision of one or two of the project contracts in each
state, and in Maharashtra, an organizational study of the
State PWD.

INDI
STATES' ROAD PROJECT
STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
I. INTRODUCTION
1.01     After a virtual hiatus of nearly twenty-five years in lending
to the roads subsector, the Bank aad India reached agreement on the
principle of having contracts for Bank-funded highway projects bid
internationally. Lending resumed with the National Highway Project
(Loan 2534-IN, 1986, US$ 200 millio.v) and the Gujarat Rural Roads
Project (Credit 1757-IN, 1987, SDR 101 million). In March 1987, the
Government of India (GOI) indicated its interest in obtaining Bank
assistance at the state road level, an intermediate category between
national highways and rural roads. The proposed project, which would
be the Bank's first operation in state roads, is consistent with the
Bank's sector strategy (para 2.16) and provides the opportunity to
pursue at that level the improvements in planning, project execution
and network management being introduced for national highways and
rural roads under the two ongoing projects.
1.02     The following criteria were established for selecting states
to participate in the project:
(a) relative need, in terms of per capita income and per capita
road density;
(b) likely economic returns from road improvements in the state;
(c) need to strengthen the state road authority; and
(d) the desirability of demonstrating the efficacy of
institutional improvements and morc appropriate construction
methods in a variety of conditions, in order to promote
replication of the project results (and the results of
expected follow-up projects in the same states) in other
parts of the country.
1.03     After due consideration, the states of Bihar, Maharashtra,
Rajasthan, and Uttar Pradesh were selected. Bihar is the poorest
state in the union, and it also has the lowest road density in
relation to population. Maharashtra is a relatively wealthy state,
with per capita income about 30% higher than the national average, but
its maintenance effort is below that of other states and it has
expressed particular interest in improving its roads administration.
Moreover, its road network is especially heavily trafficked, so that
road improvements and better maintenance in that state will yield very
good economic returns. Rajasthan's per capita income is well below
the national average, but it has one of the more progressive and
responsive road administrations, and is representative of construction



-2-
conditions encountered in the more arid states. Uttar Pradesh (UP) is
the most populous, also amongst the poorest, and with one o. the
lowest road densities. Background material on each state is presented
in Annrexes 1.1-1.4.
II. THE TRANSPORT SECTOR
A. Economic Setting and Overview of Sector
2.01     India's 3.3 million square kilometers encompasses a wide
diversity of topography and climate, ranging from parts of the
Himalayan mountains, the Indo-Gangetic plain, to the Deccan plateau of
peninsular India. About a quarter of the country's 765 million
population live in urban areas with the remainder dispersed country-
wide in some one-half million small villages. Agriculture dominates
the economy, providing about 30% of GDP and employing more than two-
thirds of the labor force. But the country also has a significant
industrial sector, at present accounting for about 27% of GDP.
Economic growth since 1980 has averaged about 5% p.a., somewhat higher
than the long-term average of 3.6%. This higher growth rate is
expected to continue, reflecting macroeconomic policy changes
introduced over the past few years. Annual per capita GNP is $300.
2.02     India's sheer size and dispersed population has shaped an
extensive transportation system comprising about 62,000 route-km of
rail lines, 1.8 million km of roads, 10 major ports, 14 major airports
(six of which handle international flights), 6,500 km of pipelines, as
well as coastal shipping and to a limited extent inl.and water
transport. Road and rail are the predominant modes of domestic
transport, accounting for about 95% of freight and passenger-km. Rail
carries a high proportion of traffic on the high-density corridors
linking the four major cities of Bombay, Delhi, Calcutta, and Madras,
ais well as bulk traffic, especially coal, on other routes. However,
road transport accounts for an increasing sh1vre of transport in these
corridors, and dominates the market for medium and shorter haul
traffic and smaller loads. Total transport demand over the past 25
years has been growing at an average annual rate of about 6%, with
higher growth rates for road transport and civil aviation.
2.03     Although the transport system, and the output therefrom, has
recorded substantial growth over the past 25 years (Annex 2.1), major
deficiencies remain and most transport modes need to modernize. The
road subsector is discussed in Chapter III. In railways, about 17,500
track-km are overdue for renewal and a further 2,000 km become due for
renewal each year. A large proportion of the locomotive fleet is
obsolete and under-powered and the outmoded rolling stock restricts
speed, thereby contributing to inefficient use of track capacity.
Outmoded terminals and yards also contribute to poor utilization of
rolling stock. Ongoing Bank-assisted projects are addressing many of
these needs.



-3-
2.04     Ports are not adequately equipped to handle the envisaged
quantities of bulks (both dry and liquid) and containers, and deeper
drafts will be required at selected ports. Capacity constraints place
a premium on improving the efficiency of port operations, which has
been low in India due to labor relation problems, poor labor
productivity. stuffing/destuffing containers inside the port areas,
obsolete equipment, vehicular congestion and poor intermodal
facilities and practices.  Progress has been made on some of these
issues in recent years, especially with the belated introduction of a
combined transport document, establishment of some inland container
depots, and the decongestion and modernization of the country's
largest port, Bombay. However, much more needs to be done. The
Seventh Plan (1986-90) appropriately provides for modernization of
facilities and equipment, and the recent Port Reforms Committee Report
has identified desirable institutional improvements, including some
degree of privatization. The Bank intends to use this report as a
basis for dialogue with GOI on a possible future port project.
2.05     Thus, a substantial task lies ahead, in terms of b'th
investment and institutional/operational improvements, if the
transport sector is to cope efficiently with the demands that will be
placed upon it ir. the coming years. These demands will take the form
not only of increased level of transport activity, but also a
substantial change in the composition of that activity, reflecting
technological developments such as containerization and bulk handling.
B.  TransRort Planning and Coordination
2.06     Responsibility for transport matters is distributed between
the central and state governments. While railways, shipping, and
major ports are exclusively under the central government's (GOI's)
authority, the central and state goJernments share jurisdiction over
highways and road transport, minor ports, and inland water transport,
though their respective roles are clearly delineated. Central/state
activities are coordinated by a Transport Development Council. The
Indian Roads Congress, a body of highway professionals from all levels
of government as well as private enterprise, offers a popular forum
(through regular meetings) for exchanges of views and experience, and
its technical publications promote improved standards of practice in
road planning, design, and other aspects of road administration.
2.07     At the central level, the responsibility for ports, shipping,
and roads falls under the Ministry of Surface Transport; there are
separate Ministries of Railways and Civil Aviation, and pipelines are
administered by the Ministry of Petroleum. In September 1985, all
transport modes except pipelines were consolidated under one Ministry
of Transport, but this arrangement was abar-doned in October 1986.
Coordination is adequately effected by: (a) a Coordination Committee
comprising the Miniscers of Railways, surface Transport, and Civil
Aviation; (b) a Committee of Secretaries from these same ministries
plus the Planning Commission, serviced by a secretariat in the
Ministry of Railways; and (c) the work of the Planning Commission
itself.



-4-
2.08     All major investment programs are assessed by the Planning
Commission before inclusion in the five-year Development Plan, from
which are derived annual plans linked to budgetary allocations.
However, the Planning Conmission's review, especially of state
investments, is at a very aggregate level, and State Planning
Departments are responsible for reviewing individual projects in the
state plans. As noted in paras 2.10 and 3.10-11, planning at all
levels needs considerable strengthening.
C. AnsDort Policy and Investment
2.09     A major review of transport policy was completed in 1980 by
the National Transport Policy Committee. Its many recommendations,
almost all of which were accepted by the Government, centered on:
(a) the need to improve transport planning by developing a
systematic methodology for projecting transport demand and
its allocation on the basis of resource costs of the
different modes of transport and by developing long-term
perspective master plans for major areas of investment;
(b) promoting the appropriate inter-modal allocation of traffic
tbrough the markec mechanism (e.g., improved pricing) rather
than through regulation and physical control; and
(c) improving cost recovery and relatedly, project execution and
productivity.
2.10     In response to (a), the Planning Commission has established a
high level Steering Committee to systematize its work on long- trm
perspective planning in transport.  The Committee has commissioned
several major studies, including origin-destination surveys of present
tr2ffic flows, transport demand forecasts for major commodities on
major corridors, development of a national transport planning model,
and a long-term technological perspective study. Reports on these
studies are expected towards the end of 1988/early 1989, and should
serve to identify more accurately the linkages with other sectors and
the respective roles of the vArious transport modes in the major
corridors. However, little progress has been made in developing
master plans in specific investment areas, even at the central level,
and planning capacity at the state level continues to be weak (para
3.11).
2.11     In response to (b) and (c), the structure of rail tarif'fs has
been improved and road transport regulations liberalized; road user
taxation is being reviewed in a Bank-financed study (para 3.08), and
cost recovery has improved in railways and ports. Indian Railways has
largely corrected past under-provision for depreciation (annual
depreciation provisions have more than quadrupled since 1982) and now
finances about half of its investment from internal resources. The
ten major ports as a gronp now internally finance about 40 percent of
their investment expenditures, whereas they incurred a net operating
deficit iL. the early 1970's. To improve project execution and the



-5-
productivity of operations, modern work methods and equipment need to
be introduced in all modes where appropriate; the specific
improvements req'Aired in the road subsector are discussed in paras
3.08 and 3.18.
2.12     The major policy challenges at this point are thus to sustain
the momenttul of the above-mentioned initiatives and extend their
application. The Bank's sector strategy (para 2.16) has been designed
accordingly.
2.13     As indicated in the following table, transport's share of
total Development Plan expenditure has declined from around 23% in the
1950's and -.arly 1960's to 12% in the 1980's. In real terms, total
transport investment grew rapidly during the first three plans, but
languished thereafter and did not surpass Third Plan levels until the
Sixth Plan (1981-85). However, investment in the road subsector has
grown steadily throughout, although its percentage share has declined.
The ongoing Seventh Plan (1986-90) provides for a 50% increase in
transport investment (though whiether this actually occurs will depend
on how well the Plan allocations, which are expressed in 1985 prices,
are protected by subsequent increases to match inflation). The focus
of the transport investment plan is on modernizing rail facilities,
augmenting main read capacity, extending the network of lower class
roads and increasing the capacity of ports to handle bulks and
containers.

D. Bank Involvement in the Sector
Past Involvement
2.14     The Bank's past involvement in the sector has been
predominantly in railways, due to the Indian authorities' preference
until recently to rely almost exclusively on local competitive bidding
in road and port projects. However, following GOI's recent decision
to entertain international competitive bidding in roads and ports, the
Bank after a long hiatu3 has recommenced lending operations in these
two subsectors, with the Nhava Sheva Port Project (Loan 2367-IN, 1984,
US$ 250 million), the National Highway Project (Loan 2534-IN, 1985,
US$ 200 million), and the Gujarat Rural Roads Project (Credit 1757-IN,
1987, SDR 101 million).
2.15     Experience with recent Bank-assisted projects in the sector
.s described in Annex 2.2.  Most of the experience with completed
projects relates to railways. However, the ongoir.g National Highway
Project has suffered substantial delays in agreeing bidding documents,
due to the implementing agencies' unfamiliarity with Bank
requirements. During the preparation of the proposed project, special
effort has been taken to familiarize the state road agencies with the
Bank requirements, and in fact, procurement arrangements for most of
the civil works are sufficiently advanced so that contracts may be
signed early in 1989.
Strategy for Future Involvement
2.16     A recent Bank sector strategy paper concluded t'iat the Bank's
efforts should focus on improving planning and project execution,
productivity, ard resource mobilization, deficiencies which tend to
apply throughout the sector. The Bank will pursue these issues in
railways, where it has a long-established relationship, and in ports.
However, the main tiirust of future Bank involvement in transport would
be to consolidate its re-entry into the road subsector, having regard
to the large and growing role of road transport (para 2.02) and the
need to improve planning processes (paras 3.10-11), the quality of
main roads (paras 3.03-04) and the efficiency with which construction
and maintenance works are executed (para 3.18). Initially, the effort
will focus on the heavily trafficked national highways and the main
road networks of the States, where the economic returns from road
improvements appear highest and where the benefits from introducing
international competitive bidding and more appropriate construction
technology appear to be greatest. The magnitude of improvements
required in the road networks themselves and in the road authorities
is such that several repeater projects are likely to be necessary at
both the national and state levels to have a sufficient impact. It is
envisaged, for example, that the four states selected for this project
would need to be the subject of follow-up operations, and that these
states would serve as mcdels for the other states. Experience gained
in improving planning and project execution would be transmitted to



-7-
other states via the Indian Roads Congress (para 2.06) and by the
experience which will be accumulated by (mostly local) consultants.
2.17     The Bank-assisted improvements will increase the utilization
of the road transport fleet, the productivity of which is likely to
further increase as it is gradually modernized in ways being
identified by an ongoing Bank-financed study (para 3.08). Resource
mobilization in the road subsector is being addressed in the same
Bank-financed study (para 3.15). The participating states would be
required to indicate within six months of the results being available
to them what actions they intend taking in response to the study's
recommendations, and to prepare an action plan to implement their
intentions.
III. THE ROADS SUBSECTOR
A. The Network
3.01     Roads in India are divided into three categories on a
functional basib: (a) the primary system consisting of national
highways; (b) the secondary system consisting of state highways and
major district roads, and (c) the tertiary system, or rural roads
consisting of other district roads and village roads. In addition,
there are 'project' roads, administered by various project agencies
(such as forestry, irrigation and electricity authorities), as well as
urban roads. National highways are under the jurisdiction of the
Central Government, which meets all expenditures on construction,
improvement and maintenance. The secondary (state highways and major
district roads) and tertiary (other district roads and village roads)
systems are the responsibility of state authorities, usually the
Public Works Departments (PWDs); however, the construction,
improvement and maintenance of tertiary roads are generally delegated
to local bodies and rural communities in the states.
3.02     The national highways are the main highways in the country
connecting major ports, adjoining countries, and linking. states'
capitals. The state highways are the main trunk roads of the states
(other than national highways), connecting important cities and
districts with national highways or highways of adjacent states.
District roads traverse each district, serve areas of production and
markets, and connect districts with highways or railways. Village
roads connect villages and groups of villages to the nearest district
road, main highway, or railway. The road length in India by
categories for 1985 is shown below.

1/ "Surfaced" defined as having bituminous, water-bound

3.03     Over the past 25 years, the total length of roads has
increased from about 700,000 km to 1.8 million km, and the length of
surfaced (generally bitumen) road from 234,000 km to 833,000 km.
However, most of this growth has been lower-class roads, and in
relation to population India's density of main roads (national and
state highways) remains one of the lowest in the world. As a
consequence, the main road network is heavily trafficked.,I/ Moreover,
the quality of the main road network is inadequate. Even for the
limited national highway network of 32,000 km (2% of India's total
road length, but carrying one-third of total traffic), one-fourth
remains single-lane; nearly 22,500 km of present two-lane road need a
second two-lane carriageway; over 16,000 km need strengthening, and
over 1,700 bridges need to be rebuilt. In all, the program to bring
national rcads up-to-date would cost about Rs 105 billion (US$ 7.7 bn)
at 1988 prices. The Seventh Plan has allocated Rs 14 billion only, to
national highways; thus, the prospect of an adequate national highway
network lies far in the future.
3.04     The deficiencies of national highways are replicated in state
highways. However, even a global estimate of the cost of improving
that network to serviceable condition and capacity is unavailable,
st/ Anexes 1.1 - 1.4 provide details of the evolution of the road
networks and road traffic in the four project states.



-9-
largely because information systems covering road inventory, road
condition and traffic data are extremely weak in the state sector.
The broad magnitude of the problem is indicated by a recent Planning
Commission estimate that only 40% of state highways are in "good"
condition whilst 35% and 25% are "fair" and "poor" respectively. The
average condition of major district roads is even worse. This
situation is attributable not so much to inadequate maintenance, but
rather to initial construction standards which are inadequate for the
traffic which the road system is now carrying. Thus, a major program
of reconstruction and improvement is required to bring most of the
network to maintainable condition. At the same time, capacity
augmentation is re½.lired: most state highways and major district roads
are still single lane.
B. Demand for Road Transport
3.05     India does not have official comprehensive data on the total
volume of road traffic (e.g., expressed in vehicle-km, ton-km, or
passenger-km performed on various categories of roads). However,
unofficial estimates (Annex 6.1, Ref. 7), based on vehicle
registrations, fuel consumption, and utilization/occupancy rates,
suggest the following long-term growth rates.
Average Annual Growth Rate
1951-87
Passenger-km                         9.6
Bus                              (10.0)
Car                               (7.5)
two-wheelers and
three-wheelers                 (17.0)
Freight ton-km                        9.3
3.06     These estimates appear reasonable, viewed against some of the
official data such as vehicle registrations, fuel consumption, and
traffic counts at specific sites. Nationally, the vehicle fleet has
grown at an average annual rate of 10.8% since 1951, and 14.2% since
1975 (Annex 3.1). Two- and three-wheelers have shown the most rapid
growth (19% p.a. since 1975); trucks have increased at 9% p.a. since
1975, buses at 7% and cars at 8%. Broadly similar patterns of growth
have been experienced in the project states (Annex 1.1 - 1.4).
Consumption of automotive fuels has increased at an average annual
rate of 8% over the past decade. The most comprehensive traffic
census data is that for national highways, which have experienced
annual growth rates varying between 8% and 20% over the past decade.



-10-
3.07     Traffic on Indian roads comprises both fast- and slow-moving
vehicles with virtually no segregation of the two, except in isolated
cases. The presence of such a traffic mix increases vehicle operating
costs because it reduces traffic speeds, increases wear and tear on
vehicles, and causes higher consumption of fuel and lubricancs, as
well as higher accident rates. Mixed traffic also reduces road
capacity and thus necessitates earlier expansion works. Another
distinctive feature of traffic on Indian roads is the large proportion
(about 70%) of trucks in total motorized traffic. The present traffic
mix on Indian highways, the high proportion of trucks coupled with
significant volumes of slow-moving traffic, are the main factors
causing road congestion and early saturation of capacity, and a high
accident rate.
3.08     The truck fleet consists mostly of large two-axle vehicles
which are inefficient as load carriers and in fuel consumption, and in
most cases are owner-modified so that they can be heavily overloaded,
with consequent detriment not only to their own durability, but much
more seriously, to road pavement life. Under the ongoing Bank-
assisted National Highway Project, a Vehicle Fleet Modernization and
Road User Charges (VFM/RUC) Study is being carried out to identify a
structure of road user charges which will encourage the gradual
introduction of more efficient vehicle designs and ensure that charges
paid by different types of user more clearly reflect the costs that
their road use occasions. The study is being undertaken by a local
consulting firm with some expatriate assistance and progress is being
monitored regularly by a Planning and Implementation Group on which
the Bank is represented. A draft final report for discussion is
expected early in 1989, and will have implications for both central
and state-administered road-user charges (para 3.15).
C. Road Administration and Planning
3.09     The Roads Wing of the Ministry of Surface Transport (MOST) is
the principal road planning authority in India. It is directly
responsible for planning the development of the national highway
system, assists the Planning Commission in the preparation of the road
component of the Five-Year and Annual Plans, and in cooperation with
state road agencies prepares, from time to time, long-term perspective
plans, the latest of which is the Road Development Plan for India,
1981-2000 (Annex 6.1, Reference 3). The latter, however, is very much
an outline plan and state road authorities are responsible for
developing their own Five-Year and Annual Plans, in cooperation with
State Planning Departments. (The national Five-Year Plan, prepared by
the central government's Planning Commission, contains only aggregate
information on state road nlans.)
3.10     Road planning in India, although improving, is still at an
early stage due to: (a) lack of an adequate database; (b) inadequate
planning techniques; (c) slow introduction of modern equipment for
monitoring road conditions and usage; and (d) lack of staff adequately
trained in economic analysis. MOST is committed to imprc,ving national
highway planning and has already undertaken several important steps,
including: (a) computerization of traffic data; (b) introducing a



-11-
computerized management information system to facilitate
administration of the national highway system;2/ (c) commissioning the
Central Road Research Institute (CRRI) to carry out a condition
inventory of the national highway system using modern vehicle-mounted
instruments; (d) preparing a computer program to provide econiomic
evaluations of highway investments; (e) preparing a manual for highway
planning to be used in the field; and (f) retaining consultants to
prepare four regional transport modelling studies to be used in the
assessment of future transport demand on the trunk route system of
each region.
3.11     Planning cripacity at the state level (which accounts for much
higher volumes of expenditure, para 3.12) is much weaker. None of the
states included in the project, for example, has an adequate master
plan for its own networks, or systematic procedures for deciding
priorities. Most do not even assemble traffic census data on a
systematic basis. There is thus a need to replicate at the state
level, the improvements which MOST is pursuing for national highways.
Such far-reaching changes in current practice will take time to
implement. However, the proposed project will initiate the process
and establish intermediate benchmarks to be accomplished by the end of
the project.
D. Road Expenditures and Finance
3.12     As noted above, national highways are financed by the central
government. The states finance virtually all work on the state
highways and major district roads, and state and local authorities
finance other roads, although in recent years there has been a
substantial input from the central government through various schemes.
Indeed, the most striking feature of roads expenditures, summarized
below, is the rapid increase in "other central" and "joint
central/state" schemes, which now account for almost 30% of total
roads expenditure, compared with less than 5% through the 1970s. Most
of this expenditure is devoted to the tertiary system, as is some of
the expenditure under the "state" heading. The Planning Commission
estimates that 40% of total roads expenditure is now devoted to rural
roads, for which there is a major program (the Minimum Needs Program)
with the objective of providing by 1990 road links to all villages
with a population of 1,500 or more, and to 50% of the villages with a
population of 1,000 - 1,499. Recently, the Commission has been
expressing concern that the tertiary network is expanding beyond the
capacity of local authorities to maintain it, and that the emphasis on
constructing new tertiary roads is resulting in the neglect of the
much more heavily trafficked secondary system. The Bank agrees with
this assessmeint, and the proposed project will be its first effort in
attempting to redress this imbalance in four states at least.
2  While MOST plans and finances all construction and mainter nce work
on national highways, execution of this work (usually by contract)
is administered by the state road authorities on behalf of MOST.

3.13     In 1968, a technical group in MOST comprising six Chief
Engineers and the Director-General, Roads Wing, formulated a set of
norms according to which road maintenance funds should be allocated.
These norms took into account traffic volumes, rainfall intensity,
local costs of materials and some other parameters. Since that
initial determination, various Finance Commissions (the body which
advises the President on revenue-sharing and grants-in-aid to the
states), have updated the norms, and although they remain a crude
instrument for fund allocation, they nevertheless do provide an order
of magnitude guide to relative maintenance needs and effort.

All India                        55
Source: Annex 6.1, Reference 6.
3.14     However, the norms can be a misleading measure of maintenance
funding requirements because, amongst other things, they are based on
outmoded maintenance methods and take no account of individual road
section characteristics; in particular, they fail to reflect that many
road sections need to be reconstructed to maintainable condition
before increased maintenance outlays can be productively utilized.
MOST has recently invited proposals from consultants to develop a road
maintenance management system for national highways. This system,
using the recently compiled road condition inventory, will be able to
identify the most appropriate reconstruction/maintenance measures for
individual road sections, estimate funding requirements, and generate
alternative strategies for alternat...ve levels of funding. The
proposed project will support the introduction of similar systems for
state highways and major district roads (para 4.12).
3.15     In aggregate, road users pay considerably more in taxes and
charges (approximately Rs 35 billion in 1985) than all levels of
government spend on roads. Nevertheless, in view of the magnitude of
future road expenditure needs, it may be necessary to raise further
revenue from road users (other sources of revenue being limited).
There are also some doubts as to whether the present structure of road
user taxation is appropriate. The Vehicle Fleet Modernization and
Road User Charges Study (para 3.08) is addressing these issues. The
draft final report of the study is expected early in 1989, and it will
have implications for state-financed roads as well as the national
network. To the extent that the recommendations of the VFM/RUC Study
are within the competence of the states to implement, it was confirmed
at negotiations that GOI will assist the states to carry out such
recommendations as have been agreed between GOI, the states, and the
Bank. In this connection, each state agreed to furnish to the Bank by
December 31, 1990, a time-bound action plan for implementing such
recommendations and arrange to implement such action plan.



-14-
E. Engineering
3.16     In India, the states' PWDs are responsible for preparing the
engineering/economic studies and designs of road projects. In many
cases, the needed studies/designs are carried out in-house, but
consultants are now often retained for the purpose. Bank experience
on this and earlier projects is that the engineering and design works
have been competently carried out and on time. Geometric design
standards in use throughout India are those prepared by the Indian
Roads Congress in IRC publications 3, 37, and 73 and are satisfactory
(Annex 4.5). Pavement design is based on the number of repetitions of
a standard 18000 lb. (8,200 kg) axle load to be sustained over the
design life selected and is formulated in line with procedures similar
to those of the UK Transport and Road Research Laboratory's Road Note
31. Structures are designed in accordance with IRC:6-1966 "Standard
Specifications and Code of Practice for Road Bridges." Both the
foregoing design procedures and loading specifications are acceptable
and in line with procedures in common use elsewhere. During loan
negotiations, assurances were obtained from Government that the
foregoing design standards arnd specifications will be followed in
implementing the project.
F. Construction
3.17     Road and bridge works in India are normally execu.ted by
contract. India is a country which is intersected by large rivers
requiring major crossing structures. Accordingly, contractors who
have specialized in bridge construction are generally well-
experienced, and some can tackle works of $100m or more. Most are
private firms, but some are state enterprises and many have been
successful in bidding for major bridge works outside India. It is
normal practice to allow contractors bidding on bridge works to submit
their own alternative designs on which they can bid as well. Although
the evaluation of such alternative designs can prolong the bid review
period, the result is often a more economic project as tte design
usually accords with the contractor's construction technology.
3.18     Compared with bridge construction, there has not been the
same scale of contract size in road works, where India's practice has
been to parcel out the construction works in small lots. Indeed, as a
matter of policy and employment generation, GOI has encouraged the
growth of labor-intensive construction through small contracts for
rural roads and has contributed heavily to the funding of such schemes
(para 3.12). Whilst the social objectives of such a policy may have
been largely achieved, the results in terms of road construction have
often been less than satisfactory. The use of some equipment,
especially fcr compaction of embankments, is essential. Where this
has not been available, or its use poorly supervised, roads have
deformed even under light traffic and have been vulnerable to storm
damage, resulting in a heavy and continuing maintenance burden.
Unlike this rural network, the national and state systems carry high
volumes of traffic and vehicle overloading is prevalent. To attain a
reasonable pavement life, therefore, and achieve sustained reductions



-15-
in vehicle operating costs, construction standards must be more rigid.
Specifications, especially for quality of materials, compaction and
surface tolerances plus the constraints of time, dictate the use of a
more diversified spread of equipment than would be needed on the low-
grade village roads. However, the contractor, who is the final
arbiter of the technology choice, will carry out the works in what 'ie
sees as the most effective and economic way. It is reasonable to
expect that with a present unskilled labor rate of Rs 26/day and a
skilled rate of Rs 50/day, he would utilize labor, where available, in
those applications which do not impede his timely completion of the
works.
G. Road Safety
3.19     The standard of safety on Indian roads is poor, and with
increasing traffic volumes and deteriorating road conditions, is
becoming worse. By comparison, the accident rate per 10,000 vehicles
in the U.K., according to a 1986 paper, was about 150, with 4
fatalities, whilst in India the rate was nearly 400 with 59
fatalities. Nearly half of all accidents involve t:avy trucks and
buses, not surprising in view of the high proportion of these types
of vehi.:le in the overall traffic stream.
3.20     Part of the problem is the low level of enforcement of the
laws governing the operation of vehicles. The traffic police force is
under-staffed and they are seldom in evidence on the main highways,
let alone the heavily trafficked state roads. Moreover, the poor
condition of many of the heavy vehicles plying the roads is one of the
main factors contributing to accidents.  Defective steering, tires,
brakes and, at night, poor and mis-directed headlights are frequent
shortcomings. In an attempt to address this vehicle factor, the
current National Highway Project is providing equipment to enable
vehicle testing stations to be set up in some of the centers of the
six participant states.
3.21     The present project ensures that the road works undertaken
meet the engineering norms of road safety in terms of sight distance,
safe passing distances, appropriate road markings and adequate warning
and informative signs. Additionally, at the request of the four
states, the project provides for the purchase of driving simulators,
alcohol breathalysers, equipment for the testing of night- and color-
blindness of drivers, and brake-testing equipment for vehicles. A
second National Highway Project under the central Ministry of Surface
Transport, and presently being prepared, will broaden the approach to
road safety.
IV. THE PROJECT
A. Rationale for Bank Involvement
4.01     As noted in para 2.16, the Bank's principal objectives in the
subsector are to:



-16-
(a) improve the planning, design and execution of construction
and maintenance work; and
(b) identify the means of mobilizing the resources needed to
implement economically desirable increases in road outlays.
4.02     The proposed project provides an opportunity 'or the Bank to
pursue these objectives at the state road level, with which it has not
been previously involved. The rationale for starting with the states
of Bihar, Maharashtra, Rajasthan, and Uttar Pradesh is explained in
paras 1.02-1.03. Follow-up projects in these states are likely to be
required to accomplish the necessary degree of improvement, especially
in planning. The strategy for replicating the project's
accomplishment in other states is explained in para 2.16.
B. Project Obiectives
4.03     The project would be the first in a series which would extend
the policy/institutional improvement begun under the National Highway
Pro-ject (Loan 2534-IN) to the State level, in particular by:
(a) promoting the use of modern maintenance management systems,
which each of the four states would put in place along the
lines of what is being instituted by MOST for national
highways; and
(b)  introducing impr>ved construction methods through larger
contracts of a sufficient individual size to encourage local
firms to mechanize, but which, when grouped, would also
attract international interest, and to introduce supervision
by consultants on se-ne of the project contracts.
The issue of mobilizing adequate resources to meet the needs of the
subsector is being addressed by a study of road-user charges presently
being implemented under the National Highway Project. Participating
states will be required to indicate by the end of 1989 what actions
they propose taking in response to the study's recommendations (para
3.15).
C. Project Description
4.04     The project comprises the following components:
(a) Civil Works (see Maps 20858, 209CO, and 20921).
(i) In Bihar, the construction of a major bridge across the
river Ganges at Bhagalpur, together with about 14 km of
approach roads, plus the strengthening and widening to
two lanes of two state roads totalling about 100 km
(Annex 1.1, paras 13-17);



-;7-
(ii)  In Maharashtra, the strengthening and widening to two
lanes of seven main roads totalling about 670 km;
(Annex 1.2, paras 23 and 27).
(iii) In Rajasthan, the strengthening and widening to two
lanes of eight state roads totalling about 850 km (Annex
1.3), and the widening to four lanes of one state road
of about 15 km; and
(iv) In Uttar Pradesh, the strengthening and widening to two
lanes of four state roads totalling about 600 km
(Annex 1.4, paras 13-17);
(b)  specialized equipment to assist the participating states in
monitoring the condition and usage of their road networks and
promoting road safety; and
(c)  staff training, technical assistance, and consultants
services which would introduce in all four states:
(i) a network management system based on soil type, pavement
condition, traffic, and other physical parameters in
order to prioritize work programs in accordance with
budget; and
(ii)  other improvements in states' highway administration.
Mi Civil Wor-ks
4.05     Road Strengthening and Widening.  In all four states, traffic
growth has outstripped the capacities of tne 2,300 km of mostly
single-lane roads selected for the civil works component of the
project, and on this ground alone their improvement would be
warranted. However, the volumes of heavy traffic, mostly overloaded,
have so distressed the pavements that their lives have been
prematurely shortened and their reconstruction is long overdue. There
are other roads in the four states in equally poor condition calling
for the same remedial measures. The selection of the package chosen
in each state, therefore, was based:
(a)  on current and forecast traffic patterns;
(b)  on the present condition of the road; and
(c)  on the development strategy, which has shaped each state's
road investment program.
4.06     In Bihar, the project includes the widening and strengthening
of two main roads, totalling abut 100 km in length, (Annex 1.1,
para 14-15). However, the major project component is the construction
of a bridge across the Ganges at Bhagalpur. This is a priority item
in Bihar's Seventh Development Plan and will significantly improve
access to the under-developed northern part of the state. There is at



-18-
present no permanent crossing of the river for 280 km from the bridge
at Mokameh to the Farakka Barrage at the West Bengal state border.
The bridge, which lies about half-way between these two structures,
will substantially reduce the distance for many trips and promote
north-south integration. Ultimately, the bridge will form part of a
proposed national highway running from the industrial belt in southern
Bihar to Jogbani near the Nepal border. Further details of the
rationale for this component are provided in Annex 1.1, especially
paras 22-23. The bridge will be about 4 km in length and have two
traffic lanes and footwalks. The river crossing itself is about
1,000m and the remainder of the structure will be in viaduct, mainly
to cross the flood plain on the northern bank. The decision to have a
lengthy viaduct on the north side was taken to avoid extensive river
training works to protect the approach embankment of a shorter bridge.
The solution adopted is the most economical of the bridge length/river
training works options studied and provides the best safety margin
against breaching through high flood.
4.07     The structure also needs to be linked to the main road
network, cal:ling for about 14 km of new two-lane road on the north
side of the river. On the south side, the 60 m wide reservation of a
disused railway provides adequate right-of-way, free from
encroachments, for the bridge approaches. However, to avoid adding
the bridge traffic to the already severely congested down-town area, a
bypass of Bhagalpur is desirable. The State Government has agreed
that, by the time the bridge is opened to traffic, it would have
completed the construction of such a bypass. During loan
negotiations, GOB agreed to furnish to the Bank by June 30, 1>89 a
time-based program for constructing the bypass, including, inter alia,
land acquisition.
4.08     In Mahazashtra; the project provides for the rehabilitation
and widening to two lanes of seven main roads, totalling about 670 km
(Annex 1.2, paras 23 and 27).
4.09     In Raiasthan, the state government has proposed the widening
and strengthening of eight state roads totalling about 850 km and the
widening to four lanes of a heavily trafficked section of 15 km of one
main road. The roads selected are not only some of the primary
transport routes of the state catering for through traffic, but they
also service areas of agricultural development where potential will be
enhanced by improved accessibility (Annex 1.3, para 27 and 31).
4.10     In Uttar Pradesh, four roads totalling about 600 km
(Annex 1.4, paras 14-18) will be upgraded in the less-developed
eastern portion of the state, as tneir present condition is seen as a
deterrent to development. Subgrade conditions are poor, with heavy
clay soils that require substantial pavement cover to carry the
forecast traffic loads. There are also many river and stream
crossings which will involve the widening of culverts and bridges.
Some land will need to be taken where a few alignment improvements
must be made, (however, there are no inhabitants to resettle), but in
general the present alignments are satisfactory.



-19-
(ii) Equipment
4.11     In line with MOST's strategy to rationalize budgetary
requests for road maintenance funds and then to apportion allocations
according to need, the four states require an objective but rapid
means of determining road condition. MOST has decided on the use of
road roughness as a surrogate for condition, and devices for measuring
roughness, or bump integrators, would provide a quick and satisfactory
method of classifying roads into different levels of budget
requirements, and the states will follow suit in using them.
Consequently, these machines are included for procurement. Other
items comprise modern traffic counting devices with inductton loops
which are permanently installed in roads, devices for weighing
vehicles in motion, mobile bridge inspeztion platforms, etc. The road
safety equipment to be procured focusses on ensuring drivers,
particularly of heavy trucks, are proficient and that vehicles' brakes
are in good condition. These items are listed in Annex 4.1.
(iii)  Training. Technical Assistance. and Consulting Services
4.12     The principal objective of this component is to establish in
each state a maintenance network management system based on: (a) an
inventory of their networks, detailing road geometry, pavement type
and condition; (b) a continuing record of traffic flows; and (c) the
local costs of remedial treatments ranging from simple pothole repair
to a full pavement reconstruction. By processing this data through a
suitable program, it is possible to determine the desirable annual
outlays to repair/rehabilitate priority sections of a network and
provide adequate maintenance, or, if implementation capacity and/or
budgetary constraints apply, to tailor the annual programs
accordingly. In short, it provides a rapid and systematic means for
management to assess and compare different operational strategies. By
the end of the project period, the inventories in the four states
should be well advanced, systematic collection of traffic data in
progress, and the first runs of the program being made. The project
provides for 80 man months of technical assistance and consulting
services for this purpose, as well as associated training (para 4.15).
During loan negotiations, assurances were obtained that each state
would undertake and complete by December 31, 1992, a Maintenance
Network Management Study by consultants and take steps to
progressively introduce a Maintenance Management System based on the
Study findings. Study Terms of Reference are at Annex 4.2.
4.13     Experience gained from the current National Highway Project
indicates that the standard PWD supervision practices, whilst probably
appropriate to the much smaller contracts customarily in use, are not
adequately responsive to the much greater demands, in terms of quality
control and contract management generally, of the large contracts for
road works now in use for the first time in India. The PWD's
supervisory zone is normally headed by a chief engineer with, below
him, a hierarchy of superintending engineers, executive engineers,
assistant engineers, technical staff, etc., and comprises a large body



-20-
of personnel, frequently changing, and with multiple points of contact
with the contractor. Close supervision of the project works is
essential if the standards and specifications agreed in their designs
are to be achieved. With this objective in view, the proje-* provides
for selected contracts (or a package of the larger contractz.r in each
state to be supervised by consultants. This work would be a logical
extension of the tasks that the Bank-financed road projects have seen
for the first time in India entrusted to consultants - those of
preparing the technical and economic studies for highway projects plus
the final engineering and bidding documents. Such an arrangement has
several advantages to commend it. Firstly, the selection process
would rely heavily on the quality and experience of the supervisory
staff the consultant would offer and their availability for the
duxition of the works.  By contrast, the Government group relies on
the qualities of the staff available at the time and their tenure is
uncertain. Secondly, the Engineer's duties and responsibilities are
clearly defined by his Terms of Reference, and the Engineer's
representative is given the authority to issue instructions to the
contractors. Given the increasing amounts of road work being offered
in large contracts, consultants in the private sector should be given
the opportunity to demonstrate its capabilities in supervising as well
as planning and designing road projects. During loan negotiations,
agreement was reached with each of the states on- (i) the principle
that one or two (or a package) of the project contracts in each state
should be supervised by consultants; (ii) the Terms of Reference for
the services needed, in which the consultant is designated as the
'Engineer' (Annex 4.3), and that the consultant will be appointed by
June 1, 1989.
4.14     In Maharashtra, the project includes a study to review the
State's PWD role, particularly in the road subsector, its defined
objectives and organization, recommend how best it should be staffed
to meet its commitments and how its resources should be structured and
deployed throughout the State in order to achieve optimal efficiency.
The study should also ascertain the budgetary needs of the recommended
organization. During loan negotiations, the Government agreed that by
December 31, 1990 it will have completed the Study, and that
thereafter it would implement those recommendations agreed with the
Bank in accordance with a time-based program.
4.15     The training needs envisaged by all four states focus on the
planning, design, and management of highway works with particular
emphasis on the fields of construction, maintenance and the
composition and management of the needed equipment holdings.
4.16     Many of the courses can be given in India at centres such as
the National Institute for the Training of Highway Engineers (NITHE),
New Delhi, the University of Roorkee, etc., but it would be of value
for some selected engineers to undertake courses of study overseas.
These individuals on their return would be expected to impart their
knowledge of modern practices in other countries to their colleagues
through the local courses. The UN's Department of Technical
Cooperation which is organizing training under the current National



-21-
Highway Project (Loan 2534-IN), has indicated its willingness to
provide the same services under this proposed project. An outline of
the proposed training program is at Annex 4.4.
4.17     Although the training needs of PWD's professionals can be met
by courses such as those proposed, there is an evident need for a
consistent approach to be taken to the training at the "grass roots"
level of the road labor supervisors. The capabilities exist in each
of the states' PWDs to organize and run appropriate courses on road
maintenance techniques and practices for road gangers and superv'_..rs,
and the project make provision for the purchase of the needed training
aids and other assistance needed to initiate such courses. During
loan negotiations, agreement was reached with each state that by
July 1, 1989 it would inform the Bank of the arrangements for training
the field staff of its PWD, the scope and frequency of the courses to
be offered, and the resources to be procured under the project to
facilitate such courses.
D.  Engineering
4.18     Final engineering of the project alternatives defined by the
feasibility studies was carried out in most cases by consultants
appointed by the State Government. In only one case was the work done
by the State PWD itself. However, all the designs and bidding
documents were competently prepared. The design standards to which
tha subprojects will be built are detailed in Annex 4.5 and are
satisfactory.

4.21     Unit costs of road works vary widely.  For example, in
Rajasthan, which has an arid climate and good subgrades, pavements can
be relatively thinner and there is less need for drainage structures.
In the northern states of Uttar Pradesh and Bihar, where rainfall is
high, subgrade conditions poor and road-building materials scarce,
thicker pavements are needed and bridges and culverts more frequent.
The impact of these conditions can be almost a doubling, in some
situations, of Uttar Pradesh's costs over those of Rajasthan.
4.22     The total estimated cost of the project (excluding about
$53.3 m equivalent of taxes and duties) is about $397 m equivalent, of
which $127 m (32%) would represent the direct and indirect foreign
exchange costs. For all the civil works components, construction cost
estimates are based on quantities from the final engineering designs,
coupled with February 1988 unit rates derived from cost factor inputs
and from recently awarded construction contracts.
4.23     The costs of equipment are based on current manufacturers'
prices including insurance and freight charges. The estimates of
training courses are based on the costs of similar programs being
implemented under the current National Highway Project. Cost
estimates of technical assistance/consulting services are based on the
man-months needed and the rates applicable as of February 1988.
4.24     Price escalation of the foreign exchange and local components
has been calculated, based on Bank estimates, as follows:

Foreign Escalation %       3         3         3          4         4
A 10% physical contingency has been included to allow for unforeseen
increases in quantities.
4.25     The direct and indirect foreign exchange component of the
civil works components of the project has been estimated on the
assumption that India remains a net importer of cement, steel, bitumen
and other oil derivatives. For road works, the foreign component is
therefore 28%, and for Bhagalpur Bridge, 40%. Some participation by
foreign contractors is expected, but as in the case of the current
National Highway Project, this will most likely take the form of joint
venture arrangements with local firms. During loan negotiations, the
project cost estimates were discussed and agreed with the Government.

the four participating states, in accordance with standard

High growth in current expenditures generally has contributed to a
deterioration in state finances and the situation has been exacerbated
recently by the widespread drought in Maharashtra, Rajasthan, and

-25-
Bihar. Bihar and Maharashtra have managed to control their current
budget situations, while the outlook remains more difficult in
Rajasthan and Uttar Pradesh. However, in all states project costs
will represent a small proportion of the states' total capital
receipts. The project should therefore not place a heavy burden on
state finances, provided restraint is exercised over the growth of
current expenditures and reasonable additional resource mobilization
is undertaken. During loan negotiations, the four participating
states agreed to furnish the local funding for the project promptly as
needed, and to report annually to the Bank details of each state's
road program for the coming year so that the Road Maintenance System
may be monitored.
G. Land Acquisition
4.28     There is little need to acquire new land for the project as
the works proposed mainly comprise the improvement of existing roads
within existing rights-of-way. However, the Ganges bridge at
Bhagalpur in Bihar will require links to the main road network on each
side of the river. On the north bank the new access road will cross a
flood plain that is seasonally inundated and on which there are no
improvements. It will then link with an existing major district road
which in turn joins a national highway. On the south bank, the
approaches to the bridge utilize the 60 m wide reservation of a
disused railroad, which is a sufficient right of way, and does not
involve the resettlement of any inhabitants.
4.29     GOI and the state governments have the power, defined the
Land Acquisition Act (revised in October 1984), to expropriate land
needed for roads. In the case of unimproved land, authority to enter
thereon can be obtained with little delay. However, where
improvements are involved, procedures can take longer, but, in
general, authority to enter on the land can be given even if the
compensation issue has not been settled. Land acquisition is not,
therefore, seen to be a factor which would delay project
implementation. Nevertheless, during loan negotiations, agreement was
reached with the states on the timing of land acquisition such that
the agreed program for project implementation would not be disrupted.

4.31     Civil works contracts will be awarded after international
competitive bidding among prequalified contractors in accordance with
the Bank's "Guidelines for Procurement." As far as is practicable,
individual contracts will be in the order of US$10 million, and
minimum packages of three contiguous contracts will be tendered
simultaneously so that both local and foreign contractors may be
encouraged to compete. Contractors may bid on one or more contracts
up to the limit of their prequalification, and awards will be made
after an evaluation which determines the arrangement of contracts most
advantageous to Government. However to take account of some
contracts which cannot reasonably be grouped with others because of
their isolated locations, provision is made for their procurement on
the basis of local competitive bidding, advertised locally, and in
accordance with procedures satisfactory to the Bank. Such procurement
could amount to about 5-6% of the value of the civil works components
and would be limited to packages valued at $5 million or less, and in
aggregate to US$12 million. The procurement has been so scheduled
that most contracts will be ready for award about the time of loan
signature. To the extent that local shopping procedures are needed,
individual purchases up to a value of $10,000 equivalent will be
sanctioned, in aggregate not to exceed $150,000 equivalent. The
highly specialized equipment to the value of US$3.5 million needed to
monitor road condition and usage is available from a limited number of
suppliers or single sources only and purchases will be made by using
limited international bidding (LIB) or dircct contracting. In order



-27-
to coordinate procurement of these items and to centralize
arrangements for obtaining the necessary import clearances, the Roads
Wing of the Central Ministry of Surface Transport will be responsible
for this project component. Also during loan negotiations, the
project implementation schedule was discussed and agreed (Annex 4.9).
4.32     All contracts for civil works, consultants' services and
equipment (about 25 in all) financed by the Bank will be subject to
prior review by the Bank before award. The Bank's standard domestic
preference provisions for goods and services procured by ICB will
apply in bid evaluptions.
4.33     Retroactive Financing.  Up to US$ 0.5 million equivalent is
recommended to cover eligible expenditures after November 1, 1987, for
costs incurred in implementing appraised project activities.
I. Project Execution and Supervision
4.34     Each of the four participating states would be responsible
for implementing its own civil works program under the project. To
this end, each would arrange for the formation of a supervisory unit
under a chief engineer with a cadre of personnel-engineers technicians
and support staff as well as equipment, laboratory facilities,
vehicles, etc. The size and composition of the supervisory unit is
determined on a common basis throughout India in accordance with the
magnitude of the works. It is anticipated that the works will be
undertaken by major contractors with some foreign firms participating
either on their own account or in joint-venture. Experience on the
current National Highway Project has shown that such contractors are
capable of much greater outputs of work than the smaller firms which
states have customarily employed in the past. However, as discussed
earlier (para 4.13), the supervisory units set up by the states tend
to be large and not adequately responsive to the needs of a large
profit-oriented contractor. Therefore, to provide a proven method of
contract supervision, each state agreed to employ consultants to
supervise one or two, or a package of the project contracts, and have
such consultants in place by June 1, 1989.
4.35     During negotiations, arrangements made in each state
concerning organization, equipment, and staff for project supervision
were discussed and agreed.
4.36     The Organizational Study of the Maharashtra PWD would be
undertaken by consultants employed by the Government of Maharashtra in
conformity with terms of reference agreed between GOM and the Bank.
The Network Management System in each of the four states would also be
undertaken by consultants engaged by the states in accordance with
terms and conditions acceptable to the Bank. MIOST would be
responsible for procurement of all equipment on behalf of the states.
During loan negotiations, the foregoing arrangements for project
implementation were discussed and agreed with GOI/states.

for other items procured locally;
(c) 100% of the total costs of consulting services; and
(d) 50% of the total costs of training.
