To facilitate disbursements, a special account would be established
for the project with an authorized allocation of $18.0 million. It is
expected that all expenditures, fully documented, be reimbursed
through this special account. Although the average period of
disbursement for loans, generally, in India is about seven years,
there is no history of disbursements for highway projects, as the
Bank's first and last operation in this field was some twenty-five
years ago and there were no other comparable projects until
Loan 2534-IN of 1985. However, as most of the bids for civil works
will have been evaluated by the time this project is presented to the
Board, the major potential delays through procurement problems will
have been avoided. Unless there are serious mishaps during
implementation, the construction contracts should not overrun and the
project should finish in five years. The project is expected to be
completed by December 31, 1994 and disbursements by June 30, 1995.
K. Accounts
4.38     The Bank is familiar with India's long-estatjished procedures
for financial control. Project audit is carried out under the
supervision of India's Comptroller and Auditor-General who reports to
Parliament through the Public Accounts Committee for central
government, and the State Accountant-General for each state. During
loan negotiations, the participating states agreed to keep separate



-29-
accounts for the project and to submit financial reports to the Bank
not later than nine months after the close of each year of project
implementation.
L. Environmental ImRact
4.39     During the construction phase, the civil works proposed under
the project will undoubtedly impinge adversely on the environment -
noise, dust, disruption to traffic flows and congestion arn the likely
short-term inconveniences. However, the completed works will have a
positive impact in the medium term - smooth riding surfaccs, increased
capacity resulting in shorter journey times, less congestion, noise
and exhaust pollution will generally enhance the environment.
4.40     In those few situations where a narrow strip of land needs to
be acquired in order to accommodate the widening of the road, no
improvements to the land except, perhaps, crops are involved and no
inhabitants will be displaced.
M. Project Reporting
4.41     Each state will be responsible for collating and preparing
quarterly progress reports on the proposed project's components. Such
reports will indicate progress made, problems encountered, together
with the remedial steps proposed, and proposed future programs of
activities. About six months after the physical completion of the
project, each state should present to the Bank a Project Completion
Report including details of its cost, general characteristics of its
execution and the accomplishment of its objectives. During loan
negotiations, agreement was reached concerning the format and content
of the reports.
N. Risk Analsis
4.42     All of the project's civil works components involve proven
technology design and construction standards, with which the executing
agencies are well familiar; technical risks of poor
performance/substandard work are minimal. The risks of implementation
delay should be small as contracts will be ready for award about the
time of loan signature. The success of the pavement management
studies which are vital to the planning capabilities of the states'
PWD will depend on the selection of a consultant experienced in the
field, and the rapport he establishes with his state counterparts.
Similar caveats apply co the organizational study of the Maharashtra
PWD and to the supervision of some construction contracts by
consultants. However, with an agreed short-list of thoroughly
experienced firms from whom to solicit proposals, the risk of
inadequate studies and contract supervision should be small.



-30-
V. ECONOMIC EVALUATION
A. Introduction
5.01     India's road network expanded rapidly to the present 1.8
million km, from the 0.4 million km of 1951 when the first Five-Year
Plan was introduced. However, most of this growth has been in the
tertiary system - the other district roads and village-access roads
rather than in the primary and secondary systems, the state and
national networks, which carry the bulk of the nation's traffic, but
which, for a variety of reasons, have grown only slowly. This project
is intended to help redress this situation by initiating at the state
level what was begun for the national network through the National
Highway Project (Loan 2534-IN, US$ 200 milion, April 1985).
5.02     The core sections of the national hirhways and state roads
networks were built some 15 to 20 years ago to standards which seemed
appropriate for traffic of that time, but which now have become
outmoded. A considerable proportion of those networks has still only
a one-lane carriageway and was designed for axle loads whirh took no
account of the overloading prevalent today. While these primary and
secondary networks have remained largely unimproved, the steady growth
performance of India has involved large increases in volumes of
traffic moving between cities, among rural areas and to/from ports and
rail heads. Not only have the numbers of vehicles increased, but also
their axle loads. Thus, although state and national highways sooner
or later carry practically all traffic generated in the country, their
physical capacity has not kept pace. Congestion has become common
place, speeds are low, accidents are frequent, and the costs of
maintaining the networks are mounting beyond the capacity of the
states to finance. To turn around this situation, it is necessary to
improve and upgrade the networks. However, the enormity of the task
and its costs are such that, at best, the country and the states can
afford only to: (a) improve a limited number of arteries at a time,
and (b) attempt to improve the methods and efficiency of their
maintenance activities to keep the existing network operating as best
as possible.
5.03     The project is designed to support these two lines of action
at the state level. It should help upgrade about 2,000 km of roads
from single-lane to two lanes and would increase their structural
strength to levels commensurate with current and expected traffic. At
the same. time, the project will introduce in the four states modern
network management techniques that should result in better and more
effective road maintenance and planning of road investments.
B. Project Benefits
5.04     Project benefits are expected to accrue principally from
reduced vehicle operating costs, passenger and goods travel time
savings, and road maintenance costs savings. In the case of road
improvements, these benefits have been quantified by means of the
Highway Design and Maintenance Model (HDM), a Bank-developed



-31-
analytical tool now in common use in many countries. Benefits from
the new Bhagalpur Pridge in Bihar have been quantified by a separate
methodology, summarized in para 5.10 and explained in more detail in
Annex 1.1, paras 26-35. In addition, the project is expected to yield
considerable benefits, which cannot be quantified, in terms of reduced
accidents, more effective maintenance activities and improved
institutional performance of the Public Works Departments of the
states involved in it.
5.05     The reductions in vehicle operating costs will initially
accrue to road transport operators, r-incipally truckers. Yet, the
competitiveness of the trucking inoustLy in India is expected to
ensure that these cost reductions are passed on to consumers at large.
Lower production and distribution costs are eventually expected to
manifest themselves in the market place. Institutional improvements
are expected to result in better management of limited public funds.
C. Economic Evaluation of Road Improvements
5.06     As in most applications of the HDM model, the existing road
conditions, the "base case," are known. Similarly, the pattern of
evolution of this base case, mostly an extension of the currently
observed situation, is also well mapped. For the states included in
the project, this involves a continuation of the standard routine
maintenance and some pothole patching every year, as is currently
done. The base case is modelled with the assistance of HDM to
estimate the resulting total operating cost of this situation. Total
operating costs are defined as the sum total of the cost of operating
the assumed volume of vehicles, their travel time costs, and the road
maintenance cost. The pattern of evolution of alternative
improvements and different maintenance strategies is modelled in turn
with the assistance of HDM. The construction costs of each
alternative are added to the resulting total operating costs, and
compared with the total cost of the base case. The difference between
the base case and that of the alternatives are the net benefits the
alternative would yield. The internal rate of return of the stream of
benefits is estimated and used to assess the relative advantage of
each alternative.
5.07     The definition of the base and project cases will vary from
one link to the next; for instance, the costs of improving width and
alignment of a road vary considerably depending on terrain, other
physical constraints and the condition of the existing road.
Construction costs have been, therefore, estimated exogenously as
explained in Chapter IV and in each of the State Annexes
(Annex 1.1-1.4). A total of 20% has been added to these construction
costs: 10% for construction supervision costs and another 10% for
physical contingencies. The parameters used to define each link
section are described in each State Annex. Maintenance unit costs
have been assumed common to all links. Similarly, the same vehicle
fleet characteristics and labor, crew, passenger and cargo delay costs
have been used in all states. Annexes 5.1 and 5.2 show the values
used.



-32-
5.08     For analysis purposes, links were broken down into somewhat
homogenous sections according to their phy3ical characteristics and
traffic volumes. Annual traffic volumes are shown as part of the
individual analysis of each State Annex. The traffic growth rates
have been differentiated by vehicle '.ype, and state-by-state, but they
all cluster around 7% per annum throughout the ten-year period the
investments are expected to last.
5.09     Financial costs have been converted to border prices by
applying uniformly a 0.8 conversion factor. In this process no
distinction was made between the domestic and foreign tradable
component of costs, although strictly this should be done.' This
procedure will yield slightly lower rates of return than otherwise
would be obtained because the tax component of imports (on average
about 25% of costs) is generally higher than 20%, and therefore, the
economic cost of imports has been slightly over-valued. Similarly, no
special distinction was made for the shadow price of non-tradables,
especially labor. While this may introduce some distortion in the
results, it is not expected to be significant since all works have
been designed to be carried out with specialized equipment, rather
than the more traditional labor intensive methods used in India.
D. Economic Evaluation of Bhagalpur Bridge
5.10     While the treatment of costs for the Bhagalpur Bridge is the
same as for the road improvements (paras 5.07 and 5.09), benefits have
been quantified by a separate methodology (Annex 1.1, paras 26-35),
because the principal benefits arise from distance reductions and from
avoiding the present transshipment from vehicle to ferry then back to
vehicle again. The bridge will reduce the distance of various origin
- destination trips by up to 252 km. The weighted average distance
reduction per trip is about 40 km for cars and buses, and 100 km for
trucks. The resulting savings in vehicle operating costs account for
about 30% of total estimated benefits in the opening year.
Transshipment savings account for just over half of total benefits in
the opening year, but become less significant later in the project
life. The improved access provided by the bridge is expected to
generate or induce new traffic, which accounts for only 5% of total
benefits initially, but gradually increases to 13%. Time savings,
quantified only for passenger work time and goods, account for the
remaining project benefits.
E. Results
5.11     The detailed results of the economic evaluations are
presented in Annexes 1.1-1.4 and summarized in Annex 5.3. The
project's overall weighted rate of return is 27%. Weighted rates of
return for the civil works in each state are 32% in Rajasthan, 43% in
Maharashtra, 20% in Uttar Pradesh, and 19% in Bihar. There are only
two road sections with rates of return below 15%, one in Uttar Pradesh
and one in Bihar; they represent 10% of total costs. There is also
one road section with a rate of return above 50%, it is in
Maharashtra; its cost is less than 1% of the total. The overwhelming
majority of subprojects have rates of return between 20% and 30%. On
the whole the project seems well-justified economically.



-33-
VI. AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATIONS
6.01     During loan negotiations, assurances were obtained from GOI/States
on the following matters:
(a) that by June 30, 1989, Bihar will have furnished to the Bank
a time-bound action plan for the construction of a bypass of
Bhagalpur such that it would be compl-ted by the time that
the Bhagalpur Bridge is opened to traffic (para 4.07);
(b) each state to undertake and complete by December 31, 1992, a
Maintenance Management Study by consultants, and take the
necessary steps to progressively introduce a Maintenance
Management System based on the Study findings (para 4.12);
(c) that Maharashtra, by December 31, 1990, will have completed
the Organization Study of its PWD, and thereafter take the
necessary steps to implement the Study recommendations as
agreed between Maharashtra and the Bank (para 4.14); and
(d) that each state, on an annual basis, will report to the Bank
its program of works in the roads subsector for the coming
year so that the progressive introduction of the Road
Maintenance Management System may be monitored (para 4.27).
6.02     Also during loan negotiations, understandings were reached
with GOI/States that:
(a)  to the extent that the recommendations of the RUC/VFM Study
are within the competence of the States to implement, that
GOI will assist the States to carry out such recommendations
as have been agreed between GOI, the states and the Bank. In
this connection, each state undertook to furnish to the Bank
by December 31, 1990, a time-bound action plan for
implementing such recommendations and take the necessary
actions to do so (para 3.15);
(b) that the services of consultants will be utilised in each
state to supervise the execution of selected project
contract(s), or a package of contracts, as agreed with the
Bank, such consultants to be appointed by June 1, 1989 (para
4.13); and
(c) that each state, by July 1, 1989, will inform the Bank of its
programs for the training of its staff who are concerned
withroad works and who are at less than professional level,
and of the facilities such as equipment, training aids,
course curricula and infrastructure that may need to be
procured under the project for the successful implementation
of such programs (para 4.17).
6.03     On the basis of the foregoing assurances and understandings
the project constitutes a suitable basis for a Bank loan of US$170
million and an IDA credit of SDR62.2 million (US$80 million
equivalent).



-34-
Annex 1. 1
INDIA
STATES' ROAD PROJECT
Bihar
A. Background
1.       Located in the northeastern part of India with a population
of 74 million, Bihar is India's second most populous state after Uttar
Pradesh; its population density of 426 per sq. km is twice the
national average. A high proportion of Bihar's population (13%)
belongs to scheduled tribes and castes. Almost 90% of the population
is rural, and approximately 80% of the workforce is engaged in
agriculture. However there is an industrial belt in the southern part
of the state, and manufacturing accounts for 13% of Bihar's net
domestic product at factor cost.
2.       Although Bihar is rich in natural resources (including rich
agricultural soils, mineralc, coal, lumber, uranium and iron), their
development has been slow.  Bihar is the poorest state in India, with
an annual per capita net domestic product of about US$120 million, 40%
below the national average. Other indicators confirm the low level of
development: hospital beds and doctors per 100,000 population are well
below the national average, as is the literacy rate (26% compared with
36%). Road density in relation to population is half the national
average. Extensive areas, mainly in north Bihar, are still isolated
due to lack of road access.
3.       Geographically, the principal features are hills in the
north, the alluvial plain of the river Ganges traversing the center of
the state, and the Chatanagpur plateau in the south.
B. TransRort System
4.       Bihar is served by road, rail, air and inland water services.
The state relies primarily on the road system, which is discussed in
the following paragraphs, for its internal transport requirements.
However, the main broad gauge electrified rail line between New Delhi
and Calcutta, the most heavily trafficked route in the country, passes
through the southern part of Bihar. This and other routes in the
south perform an important role in serving the predominately
interstate transport requirements of the mining and manufacturing
belt. However, the usefulness of the rail system for intrastate
transport is severely limited by the fact that it is broad gauge south
of the Ganges and mostly meter gauge north of the river. The state
capital Patna and Ranchi, an important industrial city in the south,
receive daily air services to New Delhi, Calcutta and other state
capitals. There is an international air service between Patna and
Katbmandu, the capital of Nepal. Bihar has several large rivers



-35-
Annex 1.1
Page 2 of 14
besides the Ganges; in total, there are 1262 kms of navigable inland
waterways, comprising 937 km of rivers and 325 km of canals. The
water-borne trade, comprising mainly grains, coal, and construction
materials, accounts for a relatively small proportion of the state's
total transport task, but seems to offer considerable scope for
expansion.
C. The Road Network
5.       As explained in greater detail in the main text, there are
three tiers of roads in India:
(a) national highways, planned and financed by the Central
Government, but constructed and maintained by the state road
authorities (generally known as Public Works Departments,
PWDs, but in Bihar's case known as the Road Construction
Department, RCD), on behalf of the Central Government, with
most construction and periodic maintenance executed by
contractors;
(b) RCD roads, comprising state highways and those district roads
for which the RCD accepts responsibility; and
(c) other roads, comprising other district roads, village roads,
urban roads and project roads, which are the responsibility
of various local administrations and project agencies.
As might be expected, data on the latter category is rather
fragmentary as it has not been centrally collected. However, as the
following figures illustrate, most of the growth in Bihar's network
has been in these lower class roads:
PrinciRal Road Network

by these figures, which relate mostly to surfaced roads; Bihar's total

road network is estiimated at over 80,000 kms. Despite the increase in
road length, Bihar's road density in relation to population is only
half the national average (for both surfaced and unsurfaced roads),
and the lowest of any state. While 95% of the RCD network is
surfaced, it is mostly single lane or intermediate width (lane and a
half); only 10% of the network is two-lane.
6.       Over the past seven years, the overall condition of the RCD
network has deteriorated. This is especially true of major district
roads; by contrast, the condition of other district roads has improved
despite a 16% increase in their length, suggesting that too high a
proportion of available resources may have been devoted to this
category:

these programs, the State Government is required to make matching
expenditures. Furthermore, the State Government has its own programs

11.      As discussed in greater detail in the main text, all states
in India are spending less on maintenance than the norms suggested by
the Eighth Finance Commission. In 1986 Bihar's maintenance
expenditures were 56% of the norms, just above the national average.
While this is hardly a satisfactory situation, the norms are an
imperfect guide to true maintenance funding requirements (though they
provide an order of magnitude guide), and bearing in mind that Bihar
is the poorest state, it can be considered to be making a reasonable
maintenance effort in relation to the other states, especially as it
increased maintenance expenditure by almost 60% in 1987:

E. Organization of Road Construction DeRartment (RCD)
12.      The Bihar RCD is responsible for planning, construction, and
maintenance of all roads in the state, although administration of most
district and local roads is delegated to local authorities. RCD is
headed by a Secretary who is responsible for overall functioning of
the various agencies involved in road development and maintenance in
the state. Day-to-day functioning of RCD is vested in the Engineer-
in-Chief-cum-Special Secretary, who is supported by a number of Chief
Engineers responsible for planning, monitoring, plant and equipment,
designs and project implementation activities at zonal level. Chief
Engineers are, in turn, supported by a number of circles, each heAded
by a Superintending Engineer who coordinates the activities of four to
five divisions. Each Division is headed by an Executive Engineer
supported by a number of Assistant Engineers and Junior Engineers
responsible for planning, designs, actual execution and monitoring of
highway projects incliding maintenance.



-40-
Annex 1.1
Page 7 of 14
F. The Project
13.      The project component relating to Bihar comprises:
(a)  Civil Works (see Map 20900)
(i) Construction of a major bridge over the Ganges at
Bhagalpur, together with about 14 km of approach ruads;
and
(ii) strengthening and widening to two lanes, two state roads
totalling about 100 km;
(b)  specialized equipment to assist RCD in monitoring the
condition and usage of its road network; and
(c)  staff training, technical assistance, and consulting services
to introduce a pavement management system, to essist in
contract supervision and other improvements in highway
administration.
14.      Bhagalpur Bridge:  At present, there is no permanent crossing
of the Ganges River for 280 km between the bridge at Mokameh and the
Farakka Barrage on the West Bengal state border. The bridge, which
falls about mid-way between these two structures will be about 4 km in
length and have two traffic lanes. The river crossing itself is about
l,OOOm and the remainder of the structure will be in viaduct, mainly
to cross the flood plain on the northern bank. The decision to have a
lengthy viaduct on the north side was taken to avoid extensive river
training works which would have been required to protect the approach
embankment of a shorter bridge. This solution is the most economical
of the bridge length/river training options studied and provides the
best safety margin against breaching through high flood. The
structure also needs to be linked to the main road network, requiring
about 14 km of new works.
15.      Road strengthening and widening:  The two roads to be
strengthened and widened are Hajipur-Muzaffarpur (51 km) and Sonepur-
Chhapra (50 km) (Map 20900). These roads were selected by RCD and the
Bihar Planning Department from a longer list of needs on the basis of
traffic levels, present road condition, and prospective developments
in the area of influence of the respective roads. The Hajipur-
Muzaffarpur road is one of the most important north-south state
highways, linking two east-west National Highways, and serving north-
south traffic out of the state capital Patna. Traffic on this road
has been growing rapidly since 1982 when a bridge was completed
linking Patna on the south bank of the Ganges with Hajipur on the
north bank. The bridge had to be widened from 2 lanes to 4, which was
completed in 1986. However, the Hajipur-Muzaffarpur road, which is
now carrying 4,600 vehicles per day, is still only intermediate width
(5.5m one and a half lanes), with a structurally inadequate pavement,
substandard and narrow bridges and culverts, and sections totalling



-41-
Annex 1.1
Page 8 of 14
2 km, which are subject to submergence during the wet season. Under
the project, the pavement will be strengthened and widened to two
lanes (7m), and the other deficiencies rectified.
16.       The Sonepur-Chhapra road, like that between Hajipur and
Muzaffarpur, is experiencing strong traffic growth following the
opening of the Patna-Hajipur bridge. The section to Chhapra forms
part of several alternative links to north-western Bihar and the
north-eastern part of neighbouring Uttar Pradesh, one of which
.connects with a project road in that state (viz., Gorakhpur-Ballia).
Sonepur-Chhapra is now carrying over 3,000 vehicles per day, yet is
only one and a half .anes wide (5.5m), has poor pavement, and some 7
km subject to submergence. The project will remove these
deficiencies.
17.       The other project components (equipment, technical assistance
and training) are the same as for the other states, and are described
in paras. 4.11-.16 of the 'ain text.
18.       The project in Bihar is estimated to cost Rs 1,436 million
(US$ ^9.0 million), as detailed below.

H. Economic Evaluation: BhagalRur Bridge
20.      A separate methodology was required for the Bhagalpur Bridge,
and is described in the following paragraphs.
Background
21.      The Ganges River traverses Bihar for a distance of over 460
km, dividing the state into two distinct parts - predominately
agricultural North Bihar covering 98,000 sq. km and the more
indtmstrial South Bihar covering 75,000 sq. km. The economic
integration of the two parts has long been constrained by inadequate
river crossings. Until recently, the state had only two crossing
sites (apart from ferries): a road-cum-rail bridge constructed in 1959
at Mokameh, and the Farakka Barrage at the West Bengal border,
separated by a distance of 280 kms. Between 1978 and 1982, two more
bridges were constructed west of Mokameh at Buxar near the Uttar
Pradesh border and at Patna, the state capital. However, the
unbridged 280 km stretch between Mokameh and Farakka remains, and it
is proposed to construct a new bridge at Bhagalpur, approximately mid
way along this stretch. Obviously, this would substantially reduce
the distance for many trips in the eastern part of the state, and
promote north-south integration in that area. Ultimately, the bridge
would form part of a proposed national highway running from the
industrial belt in southern Bihar via Purnia to Jogbani near the Nepal
border. However, the economic rationale lies not so much in benefits
to long distance transit traffic as in immediate distance reductions
within the bridge's direct area of influence, which does not depend on
road improvements other than the limited links encompassed in the
proposed project.
Proiect Area
22.      The project area (Map 20900), extending between Mokameh and
Farakka, encompasses traffic routes linking Bihar with West Bengal and
Orissa in the south and Nepal, Assam and north-eastern states to the
north. Covering 20,300 sq. km in the north and 26,200 sq. km in the
south, it accounts for 27% of Bihar's total area. The population in
t-he northern part numbers 10.2 million, and that in the southern part



-43-
Annex 1.1
Page 10 of 14
8.9 million (1981 census). In both parts, the population is
predominately rural, although there are 68 towns with populations of
more than 10,000, the largest (all over 100,000) being Bhagalpur and
Monghyr in the south and Katihar and Purnia in the north. Agriculture
is the main livelihood in both parts, but particularly so in the
north, where 86% of the workforce is engaged in agriculture, 60% of
the total area is cropped (compared with 40% in the south), and
cropping intensity is higher. The principal crops are rice, wheat and
maize, plus jute in the north. Southern Bihar (extending beyond the
project area) is quite heavily industrialized (cement, steel, silk and
cigarettes) and thf southern portion of the project area has
considerable industrial potential. Industrial estates are being
developed at Bhagalpur, Deogarh, Dumka, and Monghyr. Thus the two
parts of the project area complement each other, as reflected in the
movement of agricultural goods from the north and industrial and
consumer goods from the soutt.
TransRort Network
23.      The transport network in the project area is poorly
developed, particularly in the north which has 0.26 km of surfaced
road per 1,000 persons, compared with a state average of 0.48 and a
national average of 1.07. The north is served by a meter gauge rail
system, but traffic with the south has to be transshipped because the
south is served by a broad gauge system. Rail volumes between the two
regions are therefore small. Ferry services (map) cater to a
considerable volume of north-south traffic, but this is a relatively
high cost operation as heavy vehicles can be carried at only one
crossing point (Sahibganj); ferries at other locations can carry a
maximum of two light vehicles; most goods and passengers are
transferred from vehicle to ferry and back to vehicle.
24.      Although the project area'F road network is inadequate, it is
sufficiently well-developed to take advantage of the improved access
which the bridge would provide. National highway 31 runs from west to
east along the northern bank of the Ganges then turns north through
Purnia towards the north-east states. State highways, many proposed
for upgrading to national highways under the 1981-2000 National Road
Plan (Map 20900, and Annex 6.1 Reference 3), traverse the area. Most
relevant are the highway running along the southern river bank, that
running from the south through Dumka to Bhagalpur, and the highway
running north from near the bridge site through Madhepura and Birpur
to the Nepal border. Only 14 km of road (2 km on the south bank and
12 km on the north) need to be constructed or upgraded to link the
bridge into this network.
Traffic Analysis
25.      To measure north-south traffic in the project area,
classified traffic volume and origin-destination surveys were
conducted at three road sites and the four major ferry crossing points
(map), and more limited surveys were conducted at the two less
important ferry crossings; Indian Railways provided data on rail



-44-
Annex 1.1
Page 11 of 14
traffic over the Mokameh and Farakka bridges with origins or
destinations in the project area. Approximately 4000 vehicles per
day cross both the Mokameh Bridge and the Farakka Barrage each day.
Rail traffic across the two bridges totalled 13.9 million passengers
and 2.3 million tons in 1987. After applying a least distance matrix
to the origin-destination data it was concluded that the following
existing vehicular traffic is likely to divert to the bridge (1988
traffic levels):
Vehicles per day diverting from
Vehicle J.)=           Road      Ferry      MU       TotlRail
Car                    119         10         4       133
Bus                    115         --        18       133
Truck                  120         50         4       174
Motorcycle/scooter       -        110        -        110
Total                  354        170        26       550
26.      In addition to this existing vehicular traffic, 10,350
passengers/day and 3,630 tons/day are transshipped from vehicles to
ferries for the river crossing, then back to vehicles.  When the Patna
bridge was opened, 90% of this type of ferry traffic diverted to the
bridge. Because some of the ferry services in the influence area of
the proposed bridge are more distant from the bridge than those from
which traffic diverted to the Patna bridge, a lower diversion rate of
60% has been assumed.
27.      All this "base" traffic (i.e., traffic which would exist in
the absence of the new bridge) is projected to grow at 7% p.a. for
cars, 10% for buses and 6% for trucks and motorcycles/scooters,
reflecting historical traffic growth rates in the project area. Over
and above this "base" traffic, the improved accessibility provided by
the bridge is likely to induce new trips; this reflects the
"developmental impact" of the bridge. Again, experience with the
Patna bridge provides some guide to the likely magnitude of this
effect. An impact study revealed that within 5 years of the bridge
opening the number of trucks arriving from north of the Ganges to
deliver produce at the Musallahpur market in Patna increased from
2,129 to 6,702 per year, much more than can be explained by the
diversion of traffic from ferries. A further indication is given by
the traffic volumes on the bridge itself, which increased 60% within
three years of the bridge opening. Normal traffic growth might
account for about half of this, the remaining reflecting induced or
generated traffic. For the proposed project, it has been assumed
conservatively that inducee traffic would be equivalent to 10% of the



-45-
Annex 1.1
Page 12 of 14
"base" traffic in the opening year of the bridge and that this would
increase gradually to 30% within 5 years of the bridge opening.
28.      In summary, bridge traffic in 1994 (the opening year) is
projected to comprise:
Proiected Bridge Traffic. 1994
(vehicles per day)

Total                     1,930      193       2,123
Details of the sources of this traffic (an input for the calculation
of benefits) are provided in Table 3. To place this projected traffic
level in perspective, as already noted the Mokameh and Farakka bridges
each carry about 4,000 vehicles per day at present, which is likely to
reach 6,000 by 1994.
Cost/benefit analysis
29.      A standard conversion factor of 0.8 has been applied to
financial costs to convert them to economic costs at border prices.
(Strictly, the conversion factor should be applied to the domestic
component and the foreign component should be converted to c.i.f.
terms. However, for convenience the conversion factor has been
applied to all costs. As the tax component in imported items is
greater than 20%, this procedure tends to overstate economic costs and
thus understate the rate of return.) Annual maintenance costs are
estimated at 0.5% of initial construction costs, a common value for
structures. Benefits have been estimated directly in economic terms.
30.      Four categories of benefits have been quantified:
(a) cost savings for traffic diverted from road and rail and for
vehicular traffic diverted from ferries;
(b) cost savings for freight previously transshipped via ferries;
(d) travel time savings; and
I  N   1^ r  fit- , tnt  ir.ltid-x-t  t-r  ffir.



-46-
Annex 1.1
Page 13 of 14
31.      Savings in Vehicle Ooerating Costs   The bridge would reduce
the distance for various origin-destination trips by up to 252 kms
(Table 4). The weighted average distance reduction per trip (i.e.,
averaged over all origin-destination pairs) by vehicle category is:
Car            44.61 kms
Bus            42.24 kms
Truck          98.62 kms
These were multiplied by average vehicle operating costs per km for
representative roads in the project area (Table 5), which were derived
from the Road User Cost Study of 1982 (Annex 6.1, ref 2), suitably
updated. For simplicity, savings for traffic diverted from rail (a
very small part of the total - see Table 3) 'ere assumed to be the
same as those for diverted road traffic. For ferry traffic, savings
in the cost of ferry operations were added to the vehicle operating
costs resulting from distance reductions.
32.      TransshiRment Savings: It is estimated that by 1994 11,040
passengers/day and 3,090 tons/day of non-vehicular ferry traffic would
divert to the new bridge. In the absence of the bridge, this traffic
would be conveyed to the ferry crossing by 4 wheel drive vehicles
(needed to negotiate the extensive sandy foreshores of the river),
transshipped to ferry, then reloaded on 4 wheel drive vehicles on the
other side of the river. With the project, this journey will be
replaced by one along modern approach roads and across the bridge,
avoiding transshipment. The total saving in vehicle and ferry
operating costs is Rs 3.8 per passenger and Rs 39 per ton of freight.
33.      Time Savinys: Average journey time for the a passenger trip
diverted from road or rail will be reduced by an hour, and that for a
trip diverted from ferry will be reduced by half an hour. It is
estimated that 40% of the affected trips are work trips and 60%
leisure trips. Time savings for work trips have been valued at Rs
2.60 per hour, equivalent to half the national average wage rate of Rs
6.50 per hour after applying the standard conversion factor of 0.8.
Conservatively, no value was attributed to leisure time savings. The
total value of passenger time savings in 1994 is Rs 6.7 million. The
time value (inventory cost) of freight is estimated at Rs 0.7 per
ton/hour, and the average distance saving of 98.3 kms per truck trip
translates into a time saving of approximately 2 hours per trip. The
average truck load is 7.5 tons. Thus the value of truck freight time
savings in 1994 is 7.5 tons x Rs 0.7 x 2 hours x 176 trucks/day x 365
- Rs 674,520. The time saving for non-vehicular ferry freight is half
an hour per trip, so the annual value for this type of freight is
3,090 tons/day x Rs 0.7 x 0.5 x 365 - Rs 394,930.
34.      Benefits to Induced or Generated Traffic: As noted above, the
improved accessibility provided by the bridge can be expected to
induce new traffic equivalent to 10% of the base traffic. Following
the usual convention in transport evaluation, benefits to this traffic
are valued at half the unit rate applying to base traffic. Thus an
amount equivalent to 5% of the sum of the preceding benefits is added
for this item.



-47-
Annex -1.
Page 14 of 14
Results
35.      Taking a 20 year analysis period from the bridge's opening in
1994, the rate of return is 20.2% (Table 6). The following
sensitivity tests were conducted:
Test                           Rate of Return
Costs increased 10% (Table 7)                        18.9%
Completion delayed one year (Table 8)                19.6%
Traffic 10% less than forecast (Table 9)             18.8%
Thus the economic merit of the bridge is robust to the principal
risks.

Annex 1.2
MAHARASHTRA
Background
1.       The State of Maharashtra is located in Midwestern India on
the Arabian Sea. It borders on the north with the State of Gujarat,
on the north and east with Madhya Pradesh, and on its south are Andra
Pradesh, Karnataka and Goa. It is the third largest State of the
Union after Madhya Pradesh and Rajasthan. The State is mostly a high
plateau, except for the narrow coastal area between the Western Ghats
and the sea. Its 308,000 sq. km, about 10% of the surface of the
country as a whole, make it as large as Italy or Poland. However, with
a population of about 70 million (1985) it has about 1.2-times the
population of the former and almost twice the population of the
latter.
2.       Over 65% of the State's population live in rural areas and as
much as 20% in villages of less than one thousand inhabitants.
Nonetheless, Bombay, the State's capital, is the second biggest city
in the country (1985 population 8.2 million).
3.       Maharashtra is one of the more prosperous and richer states
of India; its 1985 per capita domestic product was almost 33% higher
than the national average (about US$260 vs. US$195) and it was
generated principally in the manufacturing sector, typically an urban
activity. The recent rapid industrialization of the State inevitably
created regional imbalances which have exacerbated linguistic
divisions within the State. When India was reorganized along
linguistic lines, the state of Maharashtra was left with four distinct
regions which are still recognized today: Marathwada (the western part
of the State which came from the former Hyderabad State), Vidarbha
(the northern section of the State which came from Madhya Pradesh),
Konkan (the coastal area between the Western Chats and the sea) and
Western Maharashtra (mostly the former Bombay Presidency). The
Government is now actively trying to remove these imbalances, by among
other actions, spreading the road construction and maintenance budget
(as well as the plan budgets of other activities) proportionately
among these regions.
The Transport System
4.       Maharashtra is served by air, rail, coastal shipping and road
transport. Air transport is used mostly by passengers and Bombay is
one of the busiest domestic and international airports of the country.
In addition to Bombay there are four main domestic airports and 13
smaller scheduled service airports in the State. The railways play an
important role in the State: there are 5,300 km of track of which over
60% are broad gauge and the rest meter and narrow gauge. The railway
is mostly used for moving bulk commodities and long distance freight.



-51-
Anne. 1.2
Page 2 of 10
Maharashtra has a long coastline and Bombay is one of the country's
main ports. In addition, however, there are 50 smaller ports strung
along the 720 km coast. Still, the State relies on its road network
for most of its transportation needs.
The Road Network
5.       There are about 154,000 km of roads in Maharashtra roughly
20% paved (either bituminous or cement) and 80% gravel (including
unpaved water bound macadam) and earth. This is only slightly below
the national average density of roads per sq.km of land surface, and
almost the average number of kilometers per inhabitant for india.
Thus, Maharashtra is about as well endowed with roads as any average
Indian state.
ROAD LENGTHS IN KAHARASHTRA

Earth                   14,390   29,511    34,020    58,115
6.       However, as opposed to many other States, the proportion of
paved to unpaved roads is relatively low (20% vs. 80% of the total
network). This, coupled with the fact that traffic has increased
substantially due to fast industrialization and improvements in rural
areas, and the inadequacy of road improvements, strengthening and
maintenance has resulted in considerable congestion and a deteriorated
network. Therefore, an important proportion of the roads now need to
be reconstructed and improved.
Traffic Volumes
7.       Traffic has increased considerably on most main, arterial
roads. Table 1 shows traffic .-olumes on national highways and major
state roads since 1980. However, it is difficult to extrapolate a
rate of growth of traffic from the information in Table 1 because of
the lack of consistency in the data. Thus, although the Maharashtra
Public Works Department has a permanent traffic counting program, it



-52-
Annex 1.2
Page 3 of 10
appears that deficiencies in the system (as can be appreciated from
Table 1) render the results somewhat useless for planning purposes.
In any event, the traffic volumes shown would be enough to exceed the
capacity of poorly constructed and maintained roads, in particular if
vehicle load restrictions are not enforced.
The Vehicle Fleet
8.       The increase in traffic over the roads is partly explained by
the increase in the number of vehicles registered in the State.
Between 1971 and 1986 the number of cars increased from 137,000 to
356,000 an average increase of 6.6% per year, buses increased by 5.9%
p.a. and the number of trucks almost tripled from 53,000 to 153,000 an
increase of 7.3% per annum.

9.       The increase in the number of vehicles was paralleled closely
by increases in fuel consumption particularly automotive gasoline,
which in the same period increased an average of 8.5% per year.
Consumption of diesel fuel, in contrast, did not grow at all between
!             1971 and 1981, but since then has also closely paralleled the increase
in the number of vehicles.  This is another reflection of the
explosion in road transport that has occurred in Maharashtra, as in
the rest of India, since the early 1980s.

Highway Administration
10.      The State Public Works Department (PWD, Chart 1) is
responsible for all highway related activity in Maharashtra including
the construction and maintenance of national highways for which the
Department acts as an agent of the Central Government of India.  The
Department is headed by two Secretaries one in charge of national
highways and Government buildings, and one in charge of state roads,
airports and helipads.  The Secretary for State Roads (Secretary 2) is
assisted by a Chief Engineer and Joint Secretary and eight Chief
Engineers in charge of the four regions (Bombay, Pune, Aurangabad and
Nagpur), Special Programs (mainly the Bombay Metropolitan Area),
Design, Architecture and Electrical. The Chief Engineers are assisted
by Superintending Engineers who are in charge of Circles and in turn
oversee executive engineers, deputy engineers and junior engineers.
In all the Department employs about 4,700 civil engineers, 130
electrical engineers, and about 1,500 technical assistants like
draughtsmen, accountants and clerks. In addition the Department
employs a large force of laborers and temporary workers.
Highway Planning
11.      As in all Indian States highway planning in Maharashtra is
guided by the objectives of the Road Development Plans elaborated by
successive conferences of the Chief Engineers of the various States.
The *7irst of those conferences produced the Nagpur Plan which ran from
1951 to 1961, the second produced the Bombay Plan which ran from 1961
to 1981 and the third produced the current attempt a- an all India
Road Plan, the Trivandum Plan which extends from 1981 to 2001.
12.      The guiding principle of these Plans has been the
preservation of India's rural economy by facilitating the development
of villages and small towns. Accordingly, the Plans have attempted to
improve village accessibility. The goal of the latest Plan
(1981-2001) is to have all villages of 500 inhabitants or more served
by an all weather road by the year 2001. The Plan also envisions to



-54-
Annex 1. 2
Page 5 of 10
have a national highway grid no larger than a 100 km per side, to have
all major traffic corridors served by expressways, and to have all
district headquarters, sub-divisional headquarters, major industrial,
commercial and tourist centers served by state highways.
13.      In addition to these objectives the State of Maharashtra
intends to eliminate, by the year 2001, its own regional imbalances
(see para. 3) as they were defined by a High Level Fact Finding
Commission appointed by the State Government. The Commission
determined the development imbalanc.e of each District. In roads this
imbalance was measured as a backlog, or extent by which a District was
behind the average availability of main roads in the State (main roads
the Commission defined as all weather, paved roads that join district
headquarters to one another, taluka headquarters to district
headquarters and taluka headquarters to one another). The State atms
to eliminate this backlog and to ensure that the same percentage of
the population is provided with main roads in all Districts. A
substantial proportion of the State's Plan funds is being set aside
for the removal of this backlog.
14.      As a result of these successive Plans there has been a
considerable expansion of lower class roads in the State (see para. 5)
at the expense of higher class roads which carry much higher volumes
of traffic and have remained in comparatively poorer condition. The
village connectivity policy has derived into a situation in which a
significant proportion of the traffic that moves over Maharashtra's
roads does so at a much higher cost than necessary.
15.      To counterbalance the above effect, there has been a notable
improvement in village connectivity:
Villages Connected   Villages NOT Connected
1961                            8,691                  27,470
1981                           28.629                   7.443
1981 population >1500           6,126                      78
population <1500          22,503                   7,365
16.      The question that needs to be answered now is whether the
benefits of this high village connectivity outweigh the disbenefits of
higher transport costs. It might be concluded that the connectivity
criteria, which gui'ded highway and road investments in the past, can
now be profitably replaced by an efficiency criteria. The effect of
such a change would be a shift in the use of resources from lower
class roads to the reconstruction and improvement of higher class
roads which already carry high volumes of traffic.



-55-
Annex1.
Page 6 of 10
Highway Expenditures
17.      Over the years, under the successive Plans a considerable
amount of resources have been invested in building up and expanding
the State's road network. In constant 1980 rupees about 3,000 million
has been spent by the Central Government and 9,000 million by the
State Government between 1951 and 1985:
Plan Period
1951-56 1965-61 1961-66 1966-69 1969-74 1974-79 1979-80 1980-85
Central   266.9   136.0    401.1  204.7   652.8    415.4  159.1    671.7
State     890.0   779.3  1,106.9  698.9 1,073.2  1,852.5  340.7  2,370.0
This is 370 million 1980 US d.llars of Central Government funds and
almost 1,160 million of State Government funds. Thus, the accumulated
road assets of the State of Maharashtra amount to over 1,500 million
of 1980 US dollars.
18.      A closer look at the pattern of State Government expenditures
in the last few years (Table 2) shows that, as a result of the village
connectivity policy, the State has invested, on average, about twice
as much on district and village roads than on state roads. This
coincides with a distribution of capital to current expenditures of
about 50% to 50%, as opposed to that of many other states where the
distribution is weighted considerably more towards current, mainly
maintenance, expenditures (in Rajasthan for example, 75% of the
State's road budget is spent on maintenance).
19.      This particular distribution of the State's highway and roads
budget is partly responsible for the current condition of the network.
Also responsible are the comparatively low design and construction
standards used in the past, and, perhaps more importantly, the lack of
adequate mechanisms to make investment and maintenance decisions.
Investment decisions in Maharashtra appear to be mostly motivated by
political interests rather than need, and maintenance decisions are
mostly guided by vague physical targets and financial norms rather
than the requirements of the network as determined by means of, for
example, a condition inventory.

20.      State highway activities are financed with motor vehicle fees
and taxes, aud taxes on freight and passengers. As shown below,
revenues collected by the State as user charges have, over the years,
been more than enough to cover all road expenditures incurred by the
State, including capital investments:

Highway Safety
21.      India has one of the worst traffic safety records in the
world, and the State of Maharashtra has by far the worst highway
safety record of India. While this is the result of many
collaborating factors, there is no doubt that poor driving habits,
lack of enforcement of vehicle safety regulations and of the traffic
code, and low highway design, construction and maintenance standards
significantly contribute to this iniquitous record. As the figures
below show, the situation has been deteriorating rather than improving
in the last few years. In as much as road traffic accidents are
related to the condition of the roads and their ability to carry the
volume of traffic they presently handle, the project should help to abate
the trend.

The Project
(a) ComMonents
22.      The project has four components in Maharashtra (i) a road
improvement and reconstruction coLponent, (ii) an equipment
acquisition component, (iii) a limited road maintenance component, and
(iv) a technical assistance and training component.
23.      The first involves the reconstruction and improvement of
eight sections of State Roads, totalling 673 km (Table 3). All of
them are in generally fair to poor condition and, given their present
traffic volumes, need intensive maintenance to keep them operating
adequately.
24.      The second component involves the purchase of specialized
equipment for road construction quality control and to monitor road
behavior and usage once the construction is finished. The latter
should ultimately promote improved maintenance practices in the State.
25.      The third component involves the preparation of a road
maintenance management system that would promote a better ultilization
of road funds generally but particularly those earmarked for road
maintenance. The system would be based on a careful monitoring of the
condition and use of the roads and based on economic and engineering
variables determine the best maintenance strategy to minimize life-
cycle costs for the road, which includes road user as well as
maintenance costs.
26.      The fourth component includes the financing of specialized
technical assistance in the field of civil works quality control,
technical assistance to carry out a review and assessment of the



-58-
Annex 1. 2
Page 9 of 10
current organization of the State's Public Works Department, and
overseas and in-house training in contract management and project
planning for PWD engineers.
27.        The total cost of the project is expected to be about Rs
1,265 million (about US$87 million) as detailed below:

(b) Obiectives
28.        Each of these components caters to a specific objective of
the State Government and has been assessed as justified by the Bank.
The first, for instance, is intended to correct inadequacies, and
improve the condition of the road network which would reduce
congestion and vehicle operating costs, make the road transport
industry more efficient and help to reduce traffic accidents by
improving road safety. The second would help with the design and
execution of road construction works and improve road maintenance
techniques. The road monitoring included in it would eventually allow
PWD to make more timely and better maintenance decisions. Similarly,
the road condition data base included in the project is intended to
help to improve maintenance decision making. It is an integral
component of any future attempt at introducing modern network
management techniques.



-59-
Annex 1.2
Page 10 of 10
29.      The technical assistance effort is intended to improve the
level of project planning, preparation and management to allow the
State PWD to carry out an acceptable and sustainable works program,
particularly of maintenance activities. The study of PND's
organization is intended to assess the changes, if any, that would be
necessary to facilitate activity planning and review, decision making,
and PWD's work execution ability. The supervision of one or two of
the project contracts by consultants (rather than PWD staff) is
intended to provide a useful standard of comparison with the normal
efforts of the PWD's supervisory unit, as it will be the first time
that major roadworks would be supervised in this way.
(c) Economic Analysis
30.      All eight roads in the project (Table 3) are in fair to poor
condition, their average roughness is 4500 mm/km and they are all over
20 years ole. All roads in the project have currently 5.5 m roadways
with shoulders; however, some of the roads edges are in such poor
shape, and shoulders have deteriorated to the point where, for
practical purposes, there are no shoulders.  All roads have traffic
volumes which would have justified their earlier improvement (Tables 4
to 11).
31.      The reconstruction designs have been standardized and all
sections will be built to the same prototype. The new pavements have
been calculated for a ten-year life, and depend'ng on the residual
life of the existing pavements the reconstruction cost of the roads
varied from one section to the next. Base costs as shown in paragraph
27, including a 10% for construction supervision and 10% civil work
contingencies, have been adjusted by means of the standard conversion
factor for India (0.8) to reflect economic costs.
32.      Benefits accruing from vehicle operating cost savings and
time savings have been quantified for purposes of assessing the
economic viability of the investment on each section. The latter
result from the reduced congestion and higher speeds that the new
roads will allow; however, only savings in working time for passengers
and goods in transit have been included; together time savings do not
amount to more than 10% to 15% of total benefits. On most sections
there will be also maintenance cost savings.
33.      The internal rates of economic return on each section are
shown on Tables 4 to 11. The average weighted rate of economic return
for all road sections in Maharashtra is 43.6%, on which basis the
overall project in the State is justified.

Annex 1. 3
RAJASTHAN
j ,d~~ackground
1.       The State of Rajasthan is located in Northwestern India on
the border with Pakistan.  It is the second largest State of the Union
with an area slightly over 342,000 sq.km., about the size of Finland
or Japan, and roughly 10% of the size of India as a whole. On the
west is the Great Indian Desert and from the Northeast to the
Southwest stretch the Aravalli hills.
2.       The State has about 37 million inhabitants, seven times the
population of Finland and one third that of Japan; it is the eighth
most populated State of India and one of the lower density States at
about 100 inhabitants per sq.km.. Over 80% of the population is rural
and most of them are engaged in agriculture. The largest city in the
State is the capital, Jaipur, with about one million inhabitants.
3.       In 1985, Rajasthan's per capita domestic product was close to
the national average (about US$165 vs. US$195) but the gap between the
national average and the State's income has been widening in the last
few years. The Development Index estimated by the Center for
Monitoring the Indian Economy, which takes into account power
generation, irrigation, transportation, communications, health,
literacy rates and other indicators, places Rajasthan nineteenth in
the country.
The Transgort System
4.       Rajasthan is served by air, rail and road transport.  Air
transportation is used mainly for passengers. Raii transportation,
which was pivotal to the development of India, is comparatively less
important in Rajasthan because the density of rail lines is low.
There are only 5,615 km of railway in the State, most of it meter
gauge, operated by the Western and the Northern Railways. The State,
therefore, relies mostly on roads for its transportation.
The Road Network
5.       There are about 52,000 km of roads in Rajasthan roughly 70%
of them paved and 30% gravel and earth. This is about half the
national average kms of road per inhabitant, and one third the average
national density of roads per sq.km. of surface. In this sense
Rajasthan is comparatively poorly endowed; however, roads in Rajasthan
are predominantly (about 70% of them) bituminous surfaced, which is
better than the average. Still, almost half the length oI the network
(45%) is single-lane, which, as in many other parts of India, have
become quite insufficient for the volumes of traffic now being
experienced.

Annex 1. 3

6.       Despite Rajasthan's comparatively better effort at
maintaining its roads, over the past years the condition of the
network has deteriorated to the extent that the great majority of
roads are now just in fair condition.
Condition of the Roads in Rajasthan
(% of Total Km)
1980            1983             1987

7.       Part of the reason is that the network was built to
geometrical and strength standards insufficient for the traffic
volumes now being experienced on most roads. The fact that a
significant proportion of the network is paved does not help because
traffic crossing and overtaking breaks off the edges of pavements,
which if not carefully maintained create uneven roads which have an
effective capacity lower than that of level gravel roads. Significant
portions of the network (national highways, and state and district
roads) need, therefore, to be reconstructed and improved.



-75-
Annex 1.3
Page 3 of 10
Traffic Volumes
8.       Traffic in the State has increased considerably on most roads
in the last few years. Table 1 shows traffic at selected counting
stations along national highways and some major state roads. Although
it is difficult to extrapolate a rate of growth from the figures in
Table 1 because of the inconsistency of the data, it is evident that
the volumes are enough to justify the improvement and increase in
capacity, particularly of one lane roads.
The Vehicle Fleet
9.       Significant increases in the number of vehicles explain in
part the higher volumes of traffic. Since 1970 the number of cars
registered in Rajasthan has increased almost threefold, the number of
buses has more than doubled and the number of trucks '?s five times
larger. The average rate of growth of the car, bus and truck fleet of
the State since 1970 is about 8% per year, which is in tune with the
growth in gasoline and diesel consumption.
STATE OF RAJASTHAN

Highwav Administration
10.      The Public Works Department (PWD) is responsible for the
planning, construction and maintenance of state, district and village
roads in Rajasthan. In addition, PWD acts as an agent of the Central
Government of India for the construction and maintenance of national
highways within the State. PWD also takes care of public buildings in
the State.
11.      The organization is headed by a State Secretary who exercises
authority through two Chief Engineers; one takes care of buildings and
national highways and is also Additional Secretary, the other, the
Chief Engineer Roads, takes care of state roads. The Roads Department



-76-
Annex 1.3
Page 4 of 10
comprises a Planning, Design, Traffic, and Quality Control Wing headed
by an Additional Chief Engineer, and another Additional Chief Engineer
heads the Districts' Wing which has eleven District Circles. Each
Circle is headed by a Superintending Engineer who has under him
executive engineers, assistant engineers, junior engineers and other
support staff like draghtsmen, accountants, and clerks. PWD's total
complement of professionals is 2,500 and non-professional support
staff are about 4,500. The Department permanently employs about
10,000 laborers although recruitment to the force stopped some years
ago and the numbers are reducing by attrition, and similar numbers of
occasional and temporary workers finrnced under various schemes of the
State as well as the Central Government.
Highway Planning
12.      Highway planning in Rajasthan, as in all States in India, is
conducted along the lines of the collective agreements reached at
periodic meetings of the Chief Engineers of all States. The first of
those meetings was in 1943 and it produced the Nagpur Plan which ran
from 1951 to 1961. The guiding principle of that Plan was
accessibility: no village was expected to be more than 5 miles from a
road by the end of the Plan.
13.      The second Chief Engineer's Conference was in 1957.  It
strengthened the accessibility concept: a village, it was said, should
be no farther than 1.5 miles of any road, and 4 miles off a paved
road, if the area was already developed; in a semi-developed area the
limits were 3 and 8 miles and in undeveloped areas 5 and 12 miles.
The resulting Bombay Plan (1961-1981), did not quite achieve the
stringent targets. In Rajasthan, though the mileage of roads built
increased significantly, the targets were not achieved.,
14.      The third Conference, which produced the Trivandum Plan
(1981-2001), replaced the accessibility concept by connectivity
criteria. The goal now is to connect all villages to one another by
the turn of the century. In addition the Plan would produce an
expansion of the national highways system to create a grid no more
than 100 km on each side.
15.      As a result of these successive Plans, village connectivity
in Rajasthan has improved as follows:
Village Size        Total   Cumulative Number Connected  Target
(number of inhabitants)  Number    1981       1984    1985       1990
1500 +               3,000    1,947    2,173    2,263    3,300
1000 - 1500          2,407    1,002    1,091    1,246    1,721
1000 -              27,598    2,338    2,693    2,813    4,313



-77-
Annex 1.3
Page 5 of 10
16.      Thus, expansion of the network in Rajasthan, as in all of
India, has been in the lower class roads, which carry comparatively
low volumes of traffic, while the higher class roads, which have
considerably more traffic, have remained largely unimproved. The
consequence of this strategy has been that the great majority of
traffic moves at much higher costs than necessary. This suggests that
the connectivity criteria which has guided the use of resources in the
recent past would be better replaced by an efficiency criteria. The
result of such a change would be a shift in the use of resources from
lower class roads to the recorstruction and improvement of higher
class roads which already have high volumes of traffic.
Highway Expenditures
17.      Under the successive State Plans considerable amounts of
resources havo been invested in the construction and expansion of the
state roads network. Measured in constant 1980 rupees, about 2,857
million have been spent between 1951 and 1985:
195156, 1956-61 1961-66 1966-69 1969-74 1974-79 1979-80 1980-85

This adds up to over 363 million of 1980 US dollars in State funds
alone. Still, it has been a relatively low, and decreasing, proportion
of overall State Plan expenditures:
1951-56 1956-61 1261-66 1966-69 1969-74 1974-79 1979-80 1980-65

million of 1980 US dollars) has been spent by the Central Governmert
in the State in building and improving the national highways and other
centrally financed schemes (like strategic roads, tribal roads,
special program area roads. command area roads and others).
18.      As mentioned above, over the past few years State
expenditures (which etxclude Central Government expenditures on
National HIghways) hiave averaged 60% for village and minor district
roads and 40% for st.ate and major district roads:

20.      Despite the relatively large maintenance effort in Rajasthan
compared with other states (roughly 75% of total yearly expenditures)
the condition of the network is only fair (para. 6). As mentioned
earlier, part of the reason is the original design standard of the
roads, but part of the problem lies in the mechanism by which
maintenance decisions are made. There appears to be no systemic
approach to deciding which roads should receive what kind of treatment
and when. There is no network approach to the problem, much less a
life-time cycle analysis of the consequences of different alternative
treatments that could be employed to maintain the roads.
21.      A detailed breakdown of the maintenance expenditures shows
that Rajasthan spends roughly as much on ordinary, routine maintenance
as it does on periodic renewal of surfacings and on extraordinary and
emergency repairs:

Routine Ordinary Maintenance      67.70     95.40    107.95    122.90
Periodic Maintenance              97.76    134.40    153.05    173.20
Emergency and Special Repairs    86.54    113.10    158.49    158.69
Total Maintenance Expenditures. 252.00    342.90    419.58    454.79
22.      This practical approach may be acceptable for lack of a
specific tool that generates a more effective use of resources.
Similarly, for budgeting purposes the only guideline currently in use
are the norms suggested by the Government of India's Eighth Finance
Commission. For lack of a better indicator this may be adequate;
however, the norm is ultimately an imperfect guide to the maintenance
requirements of the roads.
Hifhway Financing
23.      The Central Government finances national highway
construction, the Rural Landless Employment Program, the Famine Relief
Program and 50% of the National Rural Employment Program and other
centrally sponsored schemes. Other expenditures are financed by the
State with its own resources.
24.      State revenues come from (i) a sales tax on gasoline, vehicle
spares and accessories, and (ii) fees for vehicle registration,
licenses for commercial vehicles and a small fee levied on freight and
passengers. As can be seen from the following table, over the past
few years user charges have been more than enough to cover State
expenditures on roads and highways, including capital investments:
Revenues (Rs million)

Highway Safety
25.      Partly as a result of the increases in traffic, and partly as
a consequence of the comparatively low design and construction
standards used, the numbers of road accidents have increased
considerably. As shown below, fatalities resulting from these
accidents have more than doubled since 1977, when counts began to be
kept in Rajasthan:
1977   1978   1980   1981   1982
Number of Accidents           2,449  3,261  3,747  4,098  4,076
Number of Fatalities            507    828    745  1,274  1,168
Number of Injured             2,452  3,404  5,191  5,404  4,381
The Project
(a) Components
26.      In Rajasthan the project would have four components (i) a
road reconstruction and improvement component, (ii) an equipment
acquisition component, (iii) a limited road maintenance component, and
(iv) a technical assistance and training component.
27.      The first involves the reconstruction and improvement of ten
sections of State Roads totalling 868 km. All of them are generally
in poor condition and, given their current traffic levels, need
intensive maintenance to keep them operating adequately. The
reconstruction and improvement of these roads was judged preferable to
their continued intensive maintenance.
28.      The second component involves the purchase of specialized
equipment to control the quality of construction, and to subsequently
monitor road behavior and usage. This will ultimately allow to
maintain the roads better.
29.      The third component involves the preparation of a road
maintenance management system, which based on economic and engineering
criteria derived from road condition and usage, will allow to improve
the use of funds allocated for maintenance on a life-cycle basis, that
is taking into account not only maintenance expenditures but road user
costs as well.
30.      The fourth component involves the provision of consultant's
services for the supervision of one project or a package of contracts
as a demonstration of quality control assurance and contract
management. The training included in this component is geared to
improve the ability of selected PWD engineers to plan, design and
manage contracts more effectively. The training is intended to be
done both in-house and overseas.

(b) Obiectives
32.        Each of the project components caters to a specific objective
identified as desirable by the State Government and assessed justified
by the Bank. The first is intended to help the Rajasthan Government
to reconstruct and improve its roads and, thus, correct inadequacies
in the network. The works would reduce congestion and vehicle
operating cost, and make the road transport industry more efficient.
The reconstructions are also expected to help to reduce traffic
accidents and improve road safety.
33.        The second component would help with the design and execution
of road construction works and assist in improving maintenance
techniques. The information generated by the continual monitoring of
the roads would allow to make more timely and accurate maintenance
decisions.
34.        The third component is intertwined with the second in that
the road maintenance management system is an essential component of
any subsequent attempt at introducing modern network and pavement
management techniques. Ultimately the introduction of such a system is
indispensable to ensure that whatever resources are dedicated to
maintaining the roads yield the best possible results.



-82-
Annex 1.3
Page 10 of 10
35.      The fourth component is a practical approach to raising the
level of competence within the PWD so that the planning, preparation
and management of civil works contracts can be carried out at a scale
sustainable to the needs of the network and the ability of the State
to finance them.
(c) Economic Evaluation
36.      All ten road sections in the project (Table 2) are in poor
condition, their average roughness is 6000 mm/km and most are over 20
years old, although they were designed for an economic life of just 15
years. In 1985 they already had traffic volumes that would have
justified their improvement to two lane highways. Their current and
expected future traffic volumes (Tables 3 to 12) continue to do so.
All have over 500 vehicles per day and one (Udaipur - Dabok) has over
3000 vpd.
37.      The reconstruction designs have been standardized and all
road sections will be built to the same prototype. Pavements have
been calculated for a ten-year life. Still, the costs of carrying out
the works (para. 31) vary for different sections because the residual
value of the existing pavement is different for each of them. Base
costs vary between Rs 700,000 per kilometer (on the Churu - Haryana
Border section) and Rs 1.5 million pe kilometer (on Udaipur -
Chittorghar, a section that is being widenend to four lanes).
Supervision costs have bee estimated to be 10% of base costs, although
the supervision will be carried out by PWD staff, and 10%
contingencies has been added to the base costs to estimate the
financial costs of each section.
38.      Project benefits will accrue mostly from reductions in
vehicle operating costs and time savings. The latter result from the
reduced congestion that the roads will have as they are improved from
single lane carriageways to two lane roads. However, only passenger's
working time and goods in transit time have been quantified, together
time savings are no more than 10% of total benefits. On most road
sections there are also maintenance cost savings.
39.      Economic rates of return have been estimated for all roads
applying a standard conversion factor of 0.8 to the financial costs of
the civil works (base costs plus supervision costs and contingencies).
It was also assumined that at the end of their 10-year life the works
still have a residual value of 25% of the original cost. The rates
estimated are shown on Tables 3 to 12. They range from 21 % for the
Alwar - Bhiwari section to little over 45% for the Sirohi - Abu Road
section, a heavily travelled alternative to the hillier and more
congested National Highway No. 8 used by through traffic to Gujarat.
The average weighted rate of return for all ten sections in Rajasthan
is 32% and, therefore, the works are economically justified.
