Annex 1.4
INDIA
STATES' ROAD PROJECT
Uttar Pradesh
Background
1.       The state of Uttar Pradesh (UP) is situated in the northern
part of India, bordering China and Nepal. With a population of 111
million (1981 census) it is India's most populous state; its area of
294,000 sq. km makes UP the fourth largest state and its population
density of 377 persons per sq. km is almost double the national
average. About 80% of the population lives in rural areas. Rural
population density ranges from 280 to 720 persons per sq. km, the
lowest densities occurring in the northern and western parts of the
state and the highest in the east.
2.       The economy of UP is predominantly agrarian, with agriculture
contributing about half of state income and employing almost 80% of
the work force. Economic growth has been below the national rate, and
per capita net domestic product is about $150, 75% of the national
average and making UP one of the poorest states in India. There are
other indicators that UP is one of the least advanced states in India.
The literacy rate of 27% compares with the national average of 36%.
The number of doctors and health centers per 1,000 persons is among
the lowest in India; and the provision of safe potable water has been
slow. Although the state is well connected to the rest of India via
national highways, railways and airways, intrastate communication is
inadequate. In relation to population, the length of surfaced roads
is only 60% of the national average, one quarter that of nearby
Punjab, and amongst the lowest in India.
3.       The level of development varies across the state, with the
west being more industrial and enjoying higher levels of income and
public services. Eastern UP, by contrast, is one of India's
traditional poverty belts. It is industrially backward, with less
than half the state average proportion of industrial workers. Per
capita income is lower, and surfaced road density is only half the
state average. The Central and State Governments are pursuing
policies directed at ameliorating this regional imbalance by improving
infrastructure and promoting agricultural and industrial development
in the east. The proposed project roads will support this effort.
Transport System
4.       UP is well-served by some 8,600 route-km of rail, the most
important of which is the broad gauge electrified line running from
Varanasi in the south-east via Allhahabad, Kanpur and Agra then on to
New Delhi (beyond the state's western border). This line forms part



-96-
Annex 1.4
Page 2 of 10
of the main ttunk route between New Delhi and Calcutta, the most
heavily trafficked rail corridor in the country. Another heavily
trafficked east-west broad gauge route, being electrified with Bank
assistance under Loan 2417-IN, runs through the northern half of the
state through Gorakhpur, Faizabad, the state capital Lucknow then via
Bareilly to New Delhi. The state is also quite well served witn 2,740
km of national highways (Map 20921), although much of this network
requires strengthening and widening, along with bye-passes around
major urban areas. The state road network, discussed in the following
paras, is less well developed, although it is the main means of
intrastate transport. The state's principal cities, Agra, Allahabad,
Kanpur, Lucknow and Varanasi, receive daily air services to New Delhi
and other state capitals. Some minor use is made of inland waterways
for cargo from as far away as Haldia and Calcutta.
The Road Network
5.       UP now has a total road network of almost 200,000 km, 60% of
which is lower class mostly unsurfaced roads administered by agencies
other than the state road authority, the Public Works Department
(PWD). Furthermore, 75% of PWD's network consists of "other district
roads" or lower categories, and virtually all of the growth in the
network has been in this lower end of the spectrum. Between 1975 and
1987 the length of surfaced PWD roads has increased from 29,750 to
53,300 kia., again with most of this growth occurring in other district
roads and village roads (most of the higher category roads already
having been surfaced by 1975). While all of the state highways and
major district roads are surfaced, 80% and 90% respectively are still
single lane, and in view of the heavy traffic volumes (para 7) much of
this network needs to be upgraded.

6.       Since 1975, the overall condition of the PWD network has
deteriorated, reflecting inadequate initial design standards, and
failure of the maintenance effort to keep pace with the substantial
extension that has occurred in the network. This issue is addressed
by the pavement management system that is to be developed under the
proposed project.

7.       PWD conducts regular (twice yearly) 7 day classified traffic
counts at 100 points on the state highway network, in accordance with
procedures standards laid down by the Indian Roads Congress. As might
be expected in such a populous state with low road density, traffic
volumes are considerable. Most state highways carry at least 400
commercial vehicles a day, many over 1,000; substantial lengths in the
western part of the state carry 2,000 or more, and volumes as high as
5,000 commercial vehicles a day are reached towards Delhi. The
vehicle fleet has grown at an average annual rate of 13.6% since 1979,
with motorcycles, tractors and others (mostly scooters) growing
particularly rapidly. Of more relevance for rural highways, the truck
fleet has been growing at about 8% per annum.

Road Expenditures and Plans
8.       The construction and maintenance of national highways is
financed by the Central Government, although the actual work is
executed by the UP PWD, generally using contractors. Most of the cost
of works on the PWD network is met by the state Government, although
about one quarter of the outlays - all devoted to lower class roads -
is financed by the Central Government through the National Rural
Employment Program (NREP) and the Rural Landless Employment Guarantee
Program (RLEGP). Most of the cost of works on other roads is met by
district councils, other local authorities, or project agencies such
as forestry, irrigation and electricity authorities. Much of the
latter expendil:ure does not get recorded as road expenditure, at least
in centralized records. The following discussion relates only to
Central and State Government expenditures recorded as such.
9.       In real terms, tetal Central and State Government Development
(i.e., capital) expenditure on roads in UP has increased
substantially, especially over the past 10 years.

As the road lengths cited in para. 5 confirm, much of the expenditure
has been applied to extending the lower class network, especially in
recent years. Expenditure under "other" Central schemes has increased



-99-
Annex 1.4
Page 5 of 10
markedly since 1980 with the introduction of schemes such as NREP,
RLEGP, Special Problem Area Roads and Hill Area Development roads.
For some of these schemes, the State Government is required to make
matching expenditures. Furthermore, the State Government has its own
programs for district and village roads. Overall, just under 60% of
Central and State roads expenditure under the Sixth Plan was devoted
to rural roads, and this is planned to increase to 67% under the
Seventh Plan.
10.      The major roads objectives of UP's Seventh Plan are to:
(a)  meet the national Minimum Needs Program (MNP) 1990 targets of
providing a r3ad to every village with a population in excess
of 1,500 and 50% of the villages with populations between
1,000 and 1,500; this requires the construction of some 7,500
kms of roads to connect 3,200 and 800 villages in the
respective size ranges;
(b)  reconstruct 3,100 kA of distTict and village roads;
(c)  upgrade 1,000 km of major district roads to state highways
(out of a total of 2,500 km requiring such upgrading);
(d)  widen and strengrhen 2,500 kms of state highways; and
(e)  recognizing the need for adequate all-weather crossings of
the state's numerous rivers and creeks, undertake a
substantial bridge-building program.

summarized below:

12.      As discussed in greater detail in the main text, all states
in India are spending less on road maintenance than the niorms
suggested by the Eighth Finance Commission.  (These norms are a rather
imperfect absolute measure of warranted maintenance expenditures but
nevertheless provide an order of magnitude guide for assessing
relative effort.) According to a recert Planning Commission study
(Annex 6.1 Ref. 6), UP was one of the better performers, spending 66%
of the norm compared with a national average of 55%. Considering UP
is one of the poorer states, this was quite a satisfactory situation.
However, UP's maintenance expenditures have declined sit.ce then, even
in nominal terms, so the state's maintenanee effort warrants careful
monitoring. Under the proposed project, a pavement management system
will be established to more accurately assess funding requirements,
identify the most appropriate program for alternative maintenance
budgets, and monitor the effectiveness of maintenance work.

The Proiect
*           13.       The project component for Uttar Pradesh would comprise:
(a)  the strengthening and widening to two lanes of four roads:
Sonauli-Gorakhpur (93 km), Gorakhpur-Ballia (153 km),
Faizabad-Allahabad (150 km) and Allahabad-Dohrighat (208 km)
(Map 20921);
(b) specialized equipment to assist PWD in monitoring the
condition and usage of its roads; and
(c) staff training and technical assistance to introduce a
pavement management system and other improvements in highway
administration.
14.      In early 1987 UP Government commissioned consultants to
prepare a road improvement program to promote development in the
backward eastern part of the state, one of India's traditional poverty
belts (para. 3). The consultants report (Annex 6.1, ref. 39),
identified nine priority sub-projects and the four highest ranking of
these were selected for inclusion in the proposed project.



-102-
Annex 1.4
Page 8 of 10
15.      Gorakhpur, the second largest city in eastern UP after
Varanasi, is the main trading and distribution center for the north-
eastern part of the state. It is served by two national highways as
well as by rail, and is the headquarters of the North Eab,terv Railway.
State Highway 1 runs north from Gorakhpur to Sonauli, near the border
with Nepal. There is a population in excess of 2 million people in
the influence area of the Sonauli-Gorakhpur road, which also serves
transit traffic to and from Nepal. Traffic ranges from about 700
vehicles per day at the Sonauli end to over 3,000 towards Gorakhpur,
yet -,;he road is single lane (3.6m) for most of its length. Surface
condition is generally rough and bumpy, and for some 16 km there are
deep ruts and the surface has disintegrated. A total length of 15 km
needs realignment, and sections totalling 4.4 km are subject to
submergence and need to be raised. The project will widen the road to
two lanes (7m), which is well warranted by the traffic levels, and
will rectify the other design deficiencies.
16.      The Gorakhpir-Ballia road is the southern extension of State
Highway 1. Near Ballia there is a junction with an important east-
west road which to the west provides a link to Ghazipur and Varanasi
and to the east passes along the fertile banks of the Ganges and, via
a new bridge being constructed across the Ghaghara river at Manjhi,
will link with Chhapra in Bihar, which is the terminus of a project
road in that state. The Gorakhpur-BalLia road thus forms part of a
system linking Bihar and points east (including the port of Calcutta)
to Goraklbpur and, via the Sonauli-Gorakhpur project road, NRepal. The
road passes through the headquarters of three districts, with a
population totalling over 9 million and a population density 60% above
the state average. Traffic averages about 2,000 vehicles par day and
is likely to increase significantly when construction is completed of
a major new bridge acros& the Chaghara river near Belthara, about 50
km north of Ballia. This bridge (not to be confused with the above-
mentioned-bridge at Manjhi, to the east of Ballia) replaces an
existing pontoon bridge which cannot be used during flood periods.
The pavement is generally single lane, varying from 3.6 to 4.1m, and
mostly has a rough and bumpy surface. Under the preject, the road
will be widened to two lanes (7m pavement), strengthened, and
realigned/raised as necessary.
17.      Faizabad and Allahabad are both substantial cities, and
important transport nodes. National Highway 28 and several major
State Highways pass through Faizabad, which is also a railway
junction. Allahabad lies on the main rail and national highway routes
between Calcutta and New Delhi, and three State Highways radiate from
it. The Faizabad-Allahabad road (part of State Highway 9) not only
links these two cities, but also passes through two other district
headquarters (Sultanpur and Pratapgarh) and provides an important
north-south link between three heavily trafficked east-west national
highways (2, 28 and 56). Motorized traffic (excluding motor scooters)
ranges from 800 to 2,000 vehicles per day, but the road is almost
entirely single lane. Although the road has generally good alignment
and satisfactory surface condition for most of its length, pavement
strength and cross-drainage structures are inadequate. Most of the



-103-
Annex1.
Page 9 of 10
19 bridges along the road have adequate strength and width, but four
minor bridges are weak and/or narrow and need to be replaced. The
project will widen the carriageway to two lanes (7m) and rectify the
other deficiencies.
18.         The Allahabad-Dohrighat  road passes  through  three districts
with a combined population of 10 million, almost 90% of which is in
rural areas, mostly engaged in agriculture. Motorized traffic
(excluding motor scooters) ranges from 1,200 to 2,600 vehicles per
dry, but the road is mostly single lane, with a poor to fair surface,
generally inadequate pavement strength and stretches with poor
drainage subject to submergence. The project will widen the pavement
to two lanes (7m) and remove the other deficiencies.
19.         The other project components  (equipment,  technical assistance
and training) are the same as for the other states, and are described
in paras. 4.11-.16 of the main text.
20.         The project  in UP would cost Rs  2,312 million  (US$ 160
million), as detailed below.

Economic Evaluation
21.      The road improvements in UP were evaluated using the same
methodology applied in the other states, which is described in
Chapter V of the main text. The roads were divided into sections of
reasonable homogeneity in terms of physical characteristics and
traffic levels. The rates of return for the individual sections are
presented in Tables 2 to 4 they range from 12% to 25%. The average
rate of return for the project in Uttar Pradesh is 19%.

Annex 2.2
INDIA
STATES' ROAD PROJECT
Experience with Recent Bank-Assisted Transport Projects
1.        The Bank's past involvement in the sector (Table 1) has been
predominantly in railways, due to the Indian authorities' preference for
local competitive bidding in road and port projects. However, following
GOI's recent decision to entertain international competitive bidding in
roads and ports, the Bank has, after a long hiatus, recommenced lending
operations in these two subsectors, with the Nhava Sheva Port Project
(Loan 2387-IN, 1984, US$ 250 million), the National Highways Project (Loan
2534-IN, L985, US$ 200 million), and the Gujarat Rural Roads Project
(Credit 1757-'IN, 1987, SDR 101 million).
Railways
2.        Through sixteen operations approved between 1949 and 1984, Bank
Group assistance to Indian Railways (IR) has totalled US$ 1.8 billion
equivalent. The first thirteen operations were based on one- to three-
year slices of IR's investment programs and covered foreign exchange for
acquisition of: (a) materials, parts and components for manufacture of
motive power and rolling stock; (b) machinery and plant for IR manufactur-
ing units; and (c) materials and equipment for line improvement works and
telecommunications. Since 1978, there have been three specific investment
projects focussing on particular components in respect of motive power,
rolling stock, workshop and track electrification. The audit (Report
No. 6055-IN, January 10, 1986) on the Thirteenth Railway Project, the last
of the program loans, concluded that the project "came close to achieving
its principal objective to provide capacity for the forecast increase in
traffic during the Project period...  and IR's operational and financial
performance improved... However, the Project included some investments of
low economic priority while inadequate resources were devoted to higher
priority investments in replacements and renewals...  and resources allo-
cated to the Depreciation Reserve Fund were inadequate."
3.        The first of the specific projects, the Railway Modernization
and Maintenance Project (Credit 844-IN, 1978, US$ 190 million) was satis-
factorily completed in late 1985, about 18 months behind schedule. The
project had two major components: (a) support for the first phase of IR's
workshop modernization and rationalization program; and (b) the estab-
lishment of a wheel and axle plant. The audit found that the element of
workshop modernization devoted to five major pre-specified workshops was
"successful and probably justified", but that the program element of
workshops modernization, "in which machine tools were placed in numerous
shops around the country that may or may not be viable elements of the
maintenance system, probably had a much lower return." Moreover little
rationalization has occurred and few of the potential labor savings have
been realized. Regarding the wheel and axle plant, the audit noted that



-113-
Annex 2.2
Page 2 of 3
while this is a "model industrial operation," its economic impact has been
disappointing, because of cost overruns and the decline in the world
market for rail equipment. Overall, the audit concluded that any com-
ponent having a major impact on the labor force needs careful design, but
that this project and its successors (paras 4-5) have "sharply outlined
the real long-term problems facing IR and the constraints the Bank must
consider when structuring a lending operation. This was not the case with
the earlier lending format and is the useful result of a serious attempt
to effect specific changes."
4.        The Railway Modernization and Maintenance Project II (Credit
1299-IN/Loan 2210-IN, 1982, SDR 184.7 million and US$ 200 million) is
directed at (a) further improvements in the maintenance system through
establishment of a facility for reclamation of diesel electric locomotive
components and assemblies, and the provision of unit exchange spares; (b)
introduction of new electric locomotive technology; and (c) components and
materials for new design high-capacity wagons. This project is behind
schedule due to disturbances in the Punjab (the site of the Diesel
Component Works), and delays i& initiating procurement of electric
locomotives/components and bogies for wagons. However, 80X of the
Credit/Loan is now committed.
S.        The Railway Electrification and Workshop Modernization Project
(Loan 2417-IN, 1984, US$ 280.7 million) supports electrification of about
3000 route-kms, and Phase II of the workshop modernization program. After
a slow start, physical implementation is now progressing well and the
project is expected to be completed on time. About 60X of the loan has
been committed.
Ports
6.        The Nhava Sheva Port Project (Loan 2387-IN, 1984, US$ 250
million) supports the establishment of a new bulk and container port in
the Bombay area. This project also started slowly, but physical implemen-
tation is now proceeding well and is expected to be completed by June
1989, about a year behind schedule. However, institutional development is
behind schedule, which may require that initial operations are contracted
out to a port management company.
Roads
7.        The Bihar Rural Roads Project (Credit 1072-IN, 1980, US$ 35
million) comprises the construction/improvement of about 700 kms of rural
roads, and improvements to the maintenance organization (including provi-
sion of equipment). This project has been implemented satisfactorily,
though with some delays, and is nearing completion.



-114-
Annex 2.2
Page 3 of 3
8.        The National Highway Project (Loan 2534-IN, 1985, US$ 200
million) aims to (a) foster institutional development by, amongst other
things, introducing modern aspects of highway, planning, design, construc-
tion and financing; (b) evaluate the extent to whicn modern, more fuel-
efficient vehicles should be encouraged to ply Indian roads, and if so how
to promote the appropriate vehicle fleet; (c) develop the capabilities of
local contractors by exposing them to foreign competition and
collaboration; and (d) alleviate congestion on some key sections of the
national highway system, by the construction! rehabilitation of some
500 kms of road in six States. The civil works component is well behind
schedule, due to delays in finalizing tender documents, evaluating tenders
and awarding contracts. In part, these delays are attributable to the
need to familiarize the state agencies responsible for executing this
component with Bank procedures. However, most of the contracts have now
been awarded. The institutional comsponents are progressing well, although
the Vehicle Fleet Modernization and Road User Charges Study was initiated
behind schedule.
9.        As the Gujarat Rural Roads Project (Credit 1757-IN, 1987,
SDR 101 million) became effective only in August 1987, there has not yet
been sufficient implementation experience to draw any conclusions.

Annex 4.2
DRAFT TERMS OF REFERENCE FOR THE DEVELOPMENT AND IMPLEMENTATION OF
AN INTEGRATED COMPUTER-AIDED ROAD MAINTENANCE MANAGEMENT SYSTEM (RMMS)
BACKGROUND
1.        The Government  of    (state)       intends to introduce a modern Road
Nt twork Management System (RMMS) to ensure a more effective and efficient use
of its  road rehabilitation  and maintenance  funds.   At present, maintenance
decisions are largely based on (i) the judgement and experience of engineers
and administrators in the state Public Works Departments (PWDs), and on (ii)
varying, and sometimes inconsistent, sources of road condition information;
also, there is a marked absence of factual information on the consequences of
previous maintenance decisions. Further, because of high inflation, fewer
funds, and significant increase in the number of heavy vehicles, PWDs are
falling behind in their attempt to maintain their highways in a satisfactory
condition.
2.        There is  therefore a  pressing need for an efficient system to plan
road rehabilitation and maintenance on rational engineering and economic
grounds. The RMMS should meet this need through the establishment of an in-
tegrated system of data bases containing essential highway network statistics,
and of various computer-aided economic-engineering analysis systems. The RMMS
should enable the evaluation and economic optimization for the whole network,
of various maintenance and rehabilitation strategies, and allow producing
rapidly and accurately economically optimized annual, medium-term and long-
term roadworks programmes and budget estimates based on least total life-
cycle, infrastructure and user, costs.
3.        The system  should also  be capable  of producing computer-generated
roadworks designs and quantities, and generate automatically tendering docu-
ments for the roadworks.  In addition, it should provide  a means  of handling
contract monitoring and contract change orders in a simple manner, so that
funds budgeted for each roadworks contract can be readily reallocated to suit
site conditions.
GENERAL PRINCIPLES FOR THE DEVELOPMENT OF THE RMMS
Fundamental Structure
4.        The RMMS  shall be  developed as  an integrated system. and cover the
full range of activities that the highway authorities must carry out to pre-
pare and implement maintenance and rehabilitation works; i.e. policy evalua-
tion and optimization, works planning and budgeting, design, tendering and
construction monitoring.
5.        The RMMS  should comprise  a system  of fully  integrated data bases
which would be replicated  at each  administrative and  executive level.   The
information in the data bases would be accessed and used at these different



-119-
Annex 4.2
Page 2 of 10
levels, by means of analysis models three of which seem essential for the man-
agement of road maintenance and rehabilitation works:
(i)     A Planning and Programming Model (PPM) in which roadwork policies
and intervention strategies can be evaluated and optimized, and which
would produce annual, medium-term and long-term programmes and budgets
which minimize life-cycle costs.
(ii)   A Design and Tendering Model (DTM)  which, for the treatment types
determined as most appropriate in the PPM, would genernte designs and
tender documents automatically and rapidly, following annual network
field surveys.   The  system should be able to handle large programmes of
yearly maintenance works and should make it possible to handle rolling
programmes of annual works in a practical manner.
(iii)    A  Construction  Monitoring  Model  (CMM)  shich should allow to
constantly monitor the execution of maintenance and rehabilitation works
and at the same time handle contract change orders so that during con-
tract implementation funds budgeted for each roadworke contract can be
readily reallocated to suit site conditions.
6.        The data  bases for  the RMMS should be developed in such a way that
additional data items, and additional analysis modules can be added easily as
works progress  and, in  the future, as a more complex RMKS with broader func-
tions, e.g. bridgeworks or urban roadworks, evolves.
Multi-level System Compatibility
7.        THe RMKS should be developed in a way that allows  all agencies con-
cerned with the network to use the same information and a similar analysis
system, even though the depth of analysis and the outputs, and therefore the
specific reports required by each agency, may differ.  The principle of system
compatibility and replication at different administrative levels is extremely
important.   The system  should allow  simple and  rapid resimulation, for in-
stance for the higher level of authority (including lending agencies, if any)
who are charged with approving changes in work programming, design or imple-
mentation proposed by the agency responsible for the execution of the works.
RMMS Data Collection Philosophy
8.        The data requirements for the analysis models of the RMMS  should be
as simple as is consistent with reasonably reliable results.  The intention is
to make the RMKS operational as soon as possible, but not later  than the same
year in which the first field survey is carried out.
9.        The basic  parametric data  required for  running the RMMS should be
the same at the national, state and any other administrative level, and the
Consultant should ensure that the data requirements of the system he develops
match, one on one, the data used in similar systems either at the national
level by the Ministry of Surface Transport (MOST), or any of the states in the
project (Bihar, Maharashtra, Rajasthan  and  Uttar  Pradesh).    However, this
would not preclude inclusion in the data base for a specific administrative



-120-
Annex 4.2
Page 3 of 10
level, of additional data specific to that level. To further ensure that data
is compatible and uniform, a high level Committee will be created at which
each of the states and MOST will be represented.
10.       It is envisaged that the data requirements for  the RMMS  would fall
into two broad categoriess "Periodic Data" and "Routine Data". The sampling
frequencies in time and space for both sets of data need to be carefully
assessed by the Consultant as they impact decisively the cost of data collec-
tion and storage, as well as the reliability  of the  results.   Periodic Data
would be data that needs to be entered into the data base once at the time of
first setting up the system, and thereafter needs to be up-dated only occa-
sionally, as needed, say every 3 to 8 years, e.g. data reference points,
network inventory, pavement structure, axle load data, materials sources and
types, unit costs of maintenance and rehabilitation activities, vehicle oper-
ating costs, accident costs, time costs, etc. Routine Data is data that would
be collected at short regular intervals. In the execution of a given programme
of roadworks, routine data would need to be collected at three separate times,
corresponding to the operation of the three analysis modules, as follows:
(i) For PPM a few routine data items on the whole network at wide inter-
vals (per link or per km) like roughness; traffic volumes, composition
and loading; and, surface type, condition and level of distress.
(ii) For DTM data items would be required at closer intervals (200m or
lkm) for those selected portions of the network determined in PPM to
require overlay work: existing pavement layers and CBRs, deflection
measurements, surface aistress prior to the works, pavement crossfall
(camber), and a full inventory ( existing pavement, shoulders, ditches,
width of pavement, length of ditches, number and condition of culverts,
water levels and 10 year flood levels)
(iii) For CHM a larger number of items would be inventoried at close
intervals (typically 25m) for each contract package at least once during
the contract period, and at the end of the contract, for example pavement
structure review, crossfall review, surface type as built dimensions and
others.
11.       The information requirements have a critical effect on the structure
of the RMMS and therefore the Consultant is expected to carefully study and
assess this  item.   The most  cost effective  and appropriate methods (to the
needs and circumstances of the country) of collecting the information should
be recommended.   Consideration  should be  given and allowance should be made
for the future use of automated methods of data collection.
12.       The Consultant should ensure the present  and future  consistency of
the data collected (i) in itself, and should thus provide for the auditing of
the data, and (ii) with that of the data collected at the national, state or
any other administrative level operating a similar system, and should thus
closely coordinate and cooperate with any other consultant or authority,
particularly the  MOST, engaged  in a  similar effort.   The means for regular
calibration of measurement methods, and for independent auditing of the data



-121-
Annex 4.2
Page 4 of 10
in the collection and entry phases should be developed as part of the informa-
tion subsystem.
Systems Development Methodology
13.       Consideration should be given  to using  a "prototype" approach, in
which only the bare essential data items, an4 simplified prototypes of the
three analysis models are installed initially on a temporary basis while
development of the final more complex RMMS is still on-going. The purpose is
to concentrate initially, on developing the administrative and organizational
arrangements that will allow the system to work on a permanent basis, in par-
ticular the institutionalization of standardized data collection formats and
methods.
14.       The prototype  RMMS, if  adopted, should  be operational immediately
following the first annual survey of the network using the new standardized
RMMS data collection procedure, i.e. 6 to 8 months after the start of the con-
sultancy services.   The  prototypes would  use a  best-estimate overall main-
tenance strategy and approximate pavement deterioration functions based on the
accumulated subjective experience of local engineers, and therefore could be
used ahead of setting up the detailed policy evaluation model and ahead of
determining detailed pavement performance relationships. It is expected that
by running simplified prototypes (which can be programmed very quickly) ap-
proximate results are obtained quickly, and thereby confidence in and enthu-
siasm for the system is built up. The use of prototypes would facilitate de-
bugging the programs at the earliest possible stage of the RMMS development,
it would also allow an early start to RMHS training and help highway officials
to understand the more complex final RMMS.
Development Priorities
15.       The data bases and the three  basic analysis  systems (PPM,  DTM and
CMK) should be developed concurrently and as an integrated overall system.
Development of a PPM, which gives maintenance policy optimizations and budget
estimates, requires that the technical standards and methodology used for the
roadworks design be known; similarly, the DTM needs to be developed in such a
way that it accurately reflects the capabilities and capacities of the local
contracting industry, and using the CMM is only possible to the extent to
which works monitoring and design review during contract implementation are
feasible and practical.
16.       This principle of concurrent development of all the major components
of the RMMS does not mean the same development work effort is required on each
component. However, as the existing data in India is more advanced for some
components than for others, in developing the RMMS emphasis will need to be
placed on development of the modules lacking, and on the data collection and
data-base storage systems of the PPM in particular.



-122-
Annex 4.2
Page 5 of 10
NATURE OF THE SERVICES REQUIRED
17.       The  Consultant  shall  provide  a  team  of  specialists, including
highway planners, systems analysts, highway engineers  and economists.   These
specialists will work with and assist counterpart staff from the (state)
PWD to carry out the following tasks:
Development of the Data Base and Data Collection
(i)    A computer-based relational data base shall be developed  in which
all data is referenced to the link description, location (District) and
data reference points (kilometer posts) along the highway. The data base
should be capable of accepting data between each data reference point or
over several of these points.   The data  base shall  be formulated  in a
way which enables any number of additional data files to be added at a
later date, depending only on the amount of memory storage available.
The data base shall be developed to run on an IBM-AT compatible micro-
computer with a 40 MB hard disk and should be capable of providing strip
maps of data for each logical data group in the data base.
(ii)    A  data entry  program shall be developed, through which all data
entering the data base shall  be  processed.    This  program  shall have
extensive data error checking features and also data sorting and graphing
features to allow the highway officials to apply engineering judgement to
the processing of all basic field instrument readings and store in the
data bases only the data  parameters  required  for  the  analysis.   The
programme shall also be capable of automatically producing homogeneous
sublinks having essentially the same road condition within each sublink.
(iii) A series of standard forms for the collection of the Periodic Data
required for the RMMS shall be developed and the appropriate field sur-
veys undertaken to obtain any of the data items not yet already avail-
able.    Part   of the Periodic Data required for the RMMS, viz. pavement
thickness and history, is already be available for many road sections.
The Periodic Data will include original surface and layer types as well
as thicknesses and time of construction (or last reconstruction), overlay
or reseal types and the time of construction should also be collected.
The classification of such surfaces layers, overlays and reseals should
be agreed upon with PWD before the survey. The vehicle damage factors
should also be reviewed.
(iv)   Manuals should be prepared for collecting the  data in  a uniform,
standardized and consistent manner at all administrative levels engaged
in the use of an RMMS (viz. national and state levels); for Data Auditing
i.e. to ensure the consistency of the data collected in different parts
of the country and for different categories of roads; for the Data Entry
program; for maintaining and up-grading the data bases; and for the sort-
ing and processing of the data  prior to  analysis.   The Consultant will
ensure that training is provided in the use of all these Manuals to the
appropriate PWD staff.



-123-                                 Annex 4.2
Page 6 of 10
(v)    Reports which  can be  printed directly  from the  data base shall
include summaries of any data file on a per link basis. The distribu-
tion of any data parameter or combination of parameters over the whole of
the network shall also be able to be reported directly in either tabular
or graphical form.
(vi)   The Consultant shall ensure that  the physical  intervals at which
data is collected on the network is compatible with the level of analysis
in which the data will be used (PPM, DTM or  CMK) and  tailored to nature
of the traffic and type of road for which it is collected (national
highways, state highways, state  roads).   To this  effect the Consultant
shall  closely  coordinate  with  the  MOST and the other states at which
similar systems are being developed and used.
PPM Development
(i)    Literature Review.  A state-of-the-art report shall be prepared on
modern maintenance management planning systems used throughout the world,
including their  most  recent  developments.    Maintenance  planning and
budgeting systems  as currently used in    (state)   and as used or being
developed for national highways, shall be reviewcd and recommendations on
the introduction  of relevant  planning practices  to   (state)  shall be
made.
(ii)   Routine Data Collection.  Existing available routine data relevant
to the PPM shall be reviewed together with the associated data collec-
tion formats and methods and, where necessary, new standard formats and
data  collection  procedures  shall  be  developed.   Assistance with the
implementation of annual routine data collection surveys, using the new
formats and procedures, shall be provided. These surveys shall be done
on the whole of the road network and shall include road condition
surveys which shall be carried out between Data Reference Points (gene-
rally the kilometer posts along the road).   The  descriptions of defects
given in the Code of Practice for Maintenance of Bituminous Surfaces of
Highways (IRC:82-1982) should be followed as closely as possible, e.g.
the four categories of surface defects, cracks, deformations and disin-
tegrations visually estimated as the averages between Data Reference
Points, e.g. surface defects, pavement deformations, cracks, pot holes
etc. will be divided  into suitable  categories.   The criterion proposed
for all such classifications shall be agreed with PWD before being used
for detailed field surveys.
(iii) Pavement Deterioration Models. For the initial prototype version
of the PPM, approximate pavement deterioration relationships for the
various types of pavements shall be determined taking into account recent
international information and such relevant Indian information as may be
available from the series of trial strips which are being monitored by
the Central Road Research Institute (CRRI). With the available data the
Consultant will carry out an analysis to clarify environmental factors in
the pavement  deterioration models for various subgrades, and environmen-
tal zones for the classified pavement model groups.



-124-
Annex 4.2
Page 7 of 10
(iv)   Maintenance Effect Models.  Existing relevant Indian research work
on the improvement effects of maintenance shall be reviewed and compared
with other relationships around  the world.   If  additional field trials
are considered warranted, the trial sections shall be identified by the
Consultant in consultation with the PWD. The PWD will provide funds for
the laying of such trial sections but observance of the data, analysis
and study to arrive at pavement improvement functions will be by the
Consultant at his cost.
(v)      Treatment Standards.   The rehabilitation and maintenance treat-
ments to be used in the proposed RMHS must be agreed upon with the PWD.
Assessment of unit costs for each treatment type should be made and the
material, labor and equipment resources needed per unit of treatment
should be determined for the state.
(vi)    Subgrade  and Environmental  Classification.  The area covered by
the highway network shall be classified into various subgrade and en-
vironmental  zones.    Classification  systems  shall  be recommended and
agreed upon with the PWD and then a preliminary classification for each
road link shall be based on terra_n evaluation and discussions with state
and district officials.
(vii) Maintenance Cost Model. Surveys of maintenance costs/km for va-
rious pavement model groups, environmental zones and subgrade types at
different ages since construction or overlay shall be reviewed so that
these costs can be derived over the analysis period and included in the
life-cycle cost analysis.
(viii) Vehicle Operating Cost Models.   The vehicle  operating cost rela-
tionship', with roughness for representative vehicle types shall be ob-
tained from a review of Indian and international state of the art stu-
dies.
(ix)    Vehicle  Damage Factors and Growth.  The default values for these
vehicle damage factors and projected growth rates shall be reviewed.
(x)    Detailed PPM System Design and Programming.  The  various pavement
performance and cost models developed shall be used in the PPM computer
program to enable to predict traffic, pavement deterioration, pavement
condition improvement following treatment, vehicle operating costs, and
determination of the effect on road users costs and total transportation
costs of alternative maintenance strategies. The economic effects of
deferring maintenance should also be predicted. The PPM program shall be
developed to enable determination, in the first instance, of the optimum
economic maintenance and rehabilitation strategies for the network assum-
ing a 10- year life cycle. Selected economic strategies giving estimated
total works costs for the network within the available budgets shall then
be applied to the network through a screeuing process to determine both
the present and future physical and financial works requirements on the
network within the state. For the coming financial year, a programme of
works shall also be determined and the physical boundaries and budgets



-125-
Annex 4.2
Page 8 of 10
for contract  packages fixed.   The works programme  so determined shall
comprise clearly differentiated sub-programmes of rehabilitation works
and  periodic  maintenance  works.    For road sections not identified as
requiring pavement strengthening, the most economic and technically
appropriate treatment type is to be identified (e.g resealing, routine
patching, etc.). It should be possible to present the results on a state
wide basis and district-by-district.
(xi)    Manuals  and Training.   The  Consultant shall prepare a standard
routine data collection manual and a program User's Manual for PPM, and
shall ensure that all PWD counterpart staff are thoroughly trained in the
use of the system.
DTM and CMM Development
(i)    Review Indian  Road Maintenance  Practices.   The Consultant shall
review current routine, periodic and special maintenance, as well as
rehabilitation practices in    (state)   including  gang labor  and main-
tenance by contract (if any) practices. Budgets, as well as programming,
design and  tendering practices  shall be  documented.   The strength and
weaknesses the observed practices shall be documented and the scope for
introducing more efficient practices should  be  discussed.    Any inade-
quacies in the institutional framework of the PWD, which may have been
contributory to the past record of inadequate attention to the mainte-
nance needs of the road netwprk should be identified and improvements
recommended.
(ii)   Review Field Data  Collection and  Design Procedures  and Formats.
The Consultant shall review the data collection and design procedures
used in   (state)   for quantifying maintenance  and rehabilitation works
and  for  preparing  tender  documents.    On the basis of his review the
Consultant shall propose, if necessary, simple, standardized procedures
to enable a more effective and rapid collection and monitoring procedure,
and a simplified tender preparation procedure.
(iii) Standardize and improve the tendering and bid evaluation process.
The Consultant shall review and propose, if necessary, a standardized and
improved tender and bid evaluation process. The proposed process should
improve bidders' awareness of the project requirements and ensure that
any unrealistically low bid, which will tend to lead to poor construction
quality is rejected.
(iv)    The Consultant shall prepare recommendations for revisions to the
contractual practices presently being used, with the aim of giving Pro-
ject Managers greater flexibility to make changes during contract im-
plementation.
(v)    Improve central coordination and monitoring.  The Consultant shall
prepare recommendation to improve the work coordination and monitoring
process to enable deadlines to be set and met, and to increase feed-back
from the site supervision personnel to the PWD so that the standardized
specifications and other contract documents can be continually updated



-126-
Annex 4.2
Page 9 of 10
and improved in a systematic manner in the light of problems encountered
in the field.
(vi)   Standardization  and computerization  of the  monthly progress re-
ports.   The Consultant  shall propose  a simplified field administration
system that would enable project managers to effect simple rescheduling
of yearly budget requirements on all on-going contracts when required.
(vii)   Detailed DTM System Design and Computer Programming.  The Consul-
tant shall develop the DTM and CMH computer programmes based on the new
simplified field data collection, design and contractual procedures
adopted, and apply the DTM to the annual programme of roadworks (iden-
tified by  the PPM).   The automated production of tender documents shall
then be carried out by the DTM.
(viii) Detailed CMM System Design and Computer Programming.   The Consul-
tant shall develop a CMK computer program which would enable the tendered
DTM designs to be reviewed easily during contract implementation and to
determine in more detail the treatment thicknesses and quantities which
would produce the most economically viable solution for the budget, given
the bid price for each contract package. The CMM is then to be used
throughout the implementation of each roadworks contract to monitor
variations betveen the designed and the as-built works, enabling rapid
and accurate evaluation of contract change orders and of any field pro-
posals for additional budget.
(ix)    Manuals  and Training.  For both the DTM and the CMM, the consul-
tant shall prepare standard field data collection manuals and User's
Manuals and shall ensure that all PWD counterpart staff are fully trained
and competent in both how to use the systems and how to up-date or modify
them as technical standards and budgets change in the future.
18.       The software  and other  programs developed by the Consultant should
be readily implementable on microcomputers (IBM compatible), as well as on
other computer hardware as required for general PWD use in    (state)
EQUIPHEN?

Annex 4.3
INDIA
Supervision By Consultants of Road Work Contracts
Draft T-ms of Reference
Background
1.       The Government of India has applied for a loan from the World
Bank on behalf of the States of Bihar, Maharashtra, Rajasthan and
Uttar Pradesh for the strengthening and widening to two lanes of about
2300 km of single lane road in those four States through as many as
thirty separate contracts, plus the construction of a major bridge
across the river Ganges in the State of Bihar. For the first time in
dealing with road contracts in India, the States have agreed to the
supervision by independent consultants of one or two contracts, or a
package of contracts, in each State. The contracts to be executed
under the project follow the FIDIC Conditions of Contract
(International), 3rd Edition, and will have an average value of US $
10 million. Two or more contracts may be grouped together to form a
single package. Heretofore, road contracts were much smaller in value
and were supervised in toto by the PWD organisations of the States.
Some of the contracts under this project will also be supervised by
the State PWD.
2.       These Terms of Reference, however, refer specifically to
works to be executed in the State of              , where approximately
______km of presently single-lane road are to be strengthened and
widened to two lanes in         number contracts.
Objective
3.       The  objectives of introducing contract supervision by
consultants are two-fold:
(a) to demonstrate the efficacy of contract supervision by
external agents experienced in this field of work; and
(b) to promote technology transfer through joint-ventures between
expatriate and local firms so as to enhance the capabilities
of the latter to undertake similar work in future. ( To
achieve the same objective, the Employer may request the
consultant to employ suitably qualified PWD personnel as
counterparts. If it is possible to comply without detriment
to his work plan and in the knowledge that his responsibility
as the Engineer is not diminished, the consultant should be
prepared to consider such reasonable requests).



-129-
Annex 4.3
Page 2 of 6
4.       Foreign firms wishing to apply for this work will be required
to associate with local consulting firms, utilising to the extent
possible the latter's personnel and expertise. In this connection, it
shall be part of the submission that the joint venture agreement
between the foreign and local firms be provided, detailing clearly the
responsibilities assigned to the individuals from each firm proposed
for the project.
5.       Because the State PWDs are unfaniliar with  working with
consultants on contract supervision, and the present administrative
difficulties in delegating all supervisory authority, the consultant
should be aware that under Clause 2 - Power and Duties of Engineer -
of the FIDIC Conditions of Contract, the Engineer shall obtain the
specific approval of the Employer in writing before taking any of the
following actions specified in Part 1:
(a) approving subletting of any part of the Works under Clause 4
of Part 1;
(b) certifying additional sums under subelause 5(2) of Part 1;
(c) certifying additional cost under Clause 12 of Part 1;
(d) determining an extension of time under Clause 44 of Part 1;
(e) issuing a variation order under Clause 51 of Part 1; and
(f) fixing rates or prices under Clause 52 of Part 1.
6.       Moreover, the Employer would require prior consultation
before the Engineer orders any tests under subelause 36(4) of Part 1.

Scope of Work
(A)  Duties and Responsibilities of the Engineer
7.       The duties of the Engineer are to supervise the works and to
approve the materials and workmanship of the works in cooperation and
consultation with the Employer. He shall have no authority to relieve
the Contractor of any of his duties or obligations under the Contract
nor to order any work entailing delays or any extra payment by the
Employer.
8.       Accordingly, and in consultation with the Employer, the
principal responsibilities of the Engineer shall be:
(a) to issue the order to commence to the Contractor;
(b) to authorise the Contractor's subletting parts of the work;
(c) to explain and/or adjust ambiguities and/or discrepancies in
the Contract Documents and to provide assistance to the
Employer in the settlement of disputes with the Contractor;
(d) to approve the Contractor's working drawings and if necessary
to issue further drawings and/or to give instructions to the
Contractor;
(e) to approve data for the setting out of work;
(f) to approve or to disapprove the Contractor's superintendence,
key persorrnel and/or construction program, land to be
occupied by the Contractor, materials and/or sources of
materials;
(g) as required, to order special tests of materials and
completed works and/or removal and substitution of improper
materials and/or of work after prior consultation with the
Employer;

the suspension of Works, and to authorise extensions of the

prior approval of the Employer, and/or to make

-138-
Annex 4.8
INDIA
STATES' ROAD PROJECT
State Finances
1.       The states play an important role in the social and economic
development of India, discharging major responsibilities in the areas of
education, health, social welfare, agriculture, irrigation, roads, and
internal law and order. Resources for financing state expenditures come
mainly from tax and non-tax levies imposed by the states ("own" tax and
non-tax receipts), and from central transfers.
2.       The states levy about ten different taxes, including a sales tax;
stamp duties; taxes on motor vehicles, goods and passengers; on
entertainment tax; excise duties on liquor; and a land tax. Broad-based
sales taxes dominate state tax structures, providing 50-60% of revenues
from the states' taxes. Non-tax receipts on the current budget make a
limited contribution to revenues because the Indian states have not relied
extensively on charges for public services (user charges). On the capital
budget, the main sources of the states' own capital receipts are market
borrowing, net contributions to state provident funds, recoveries of loans
and advances made in the past, and surpluses of various reserve funds.
3.       Central transfers to the states take the form of revenue sharing,
grants-in-aid, small savings loans, and plan assistance. Revenue sharing,
which is based on the recommendations of Finance Commissions, presently
consists of a transfer by the Central Government of 45% of the net proceeds
from Union excise duties and 85% of the net proceeds from personal income
tax (but not corporation tax) to State Governments. The formulae for the
distribution of these revenues among the states are slightly different, but
both formulae take accour.t of population and per capita income. Grants-in-
aid are paid largely to states which are expected to have current budget
deficits after taking account of the devolution of revenues proposed by a
Finance Commission. Through small savings loans, the Central Government
shares with the states the resources generated from the operation of small
savings schemes.
4.       Plan assistance is provided in the form of block (general purpose)
loans and grants, assistance for specific centrally sponsored schemes, and
transfers in respect of external assistance for state projects. Block
assistance is allocated among the states on the basis of the so-called
modified Gadgil formula, which includes factors such as population, per
capita income and tax effort. Assistance for centrally sponsored plan
schemes consists of full or partial funding (matching grants) by the
Central Government of specific schemes and programs of national importance.
5.       Transfers in respect of external assistance for state projects are
in addition to transfers effected through these means. However, only a
proportion of external assistance for state projects is transmitted to the



-139-
Annex 4.8
Page2 of 16
states. Seventy percent of the proceeds from external assistance for a
state project are passed on to the relevant state, with the Center
retaining the balance. The states must therefore finance substantial
proportions of total project costs of externally aided projects from other
sources (i.e., from own capital receipts and untied central assistance).
The rationale for withholding part of the proceeds is to moderate the
effect of aid resources on the pattern of interstate allocations secured
through the Gadgil formula and transfers for centrally sponsored schemes.
6.       The project covers states with highly disparate levels of
development. It includes three states with per capita incomes below the
national average (including the poorest state, Bihar) as well as one of the
most developed states fXaharashtra). The wide variations among the states
in relative tax effor'., dependence on central transfers and in levels of
state expenditures (Table 1) reflect in part the large differences in
income levels. The disparities are the greatest in respect of receipts
from taxes controlled by the states: in per capita terms, Bihar raises Rs
75 from own tdxes, as compared to Rs 344 raised by Maharashtra. Central
transfers, on the other hand, are relatively more important in poorer
states -- for example, the- finance two-thirds of Bihar's total
expenditures, but just over one-quarter of those of Maharashtra. Despite
this, large dlfferences in per capita state expenditures persist.
Overall Trends
7.       The financial positions of the project states should be seen in
the context of trends itt overall state finances. State finances have
deteriorated in recent years. High growth in current expenditures has been
a major reason for this. An important indicator of growing pressures on
state resources is the trend in current budget balances. The substantial
current budget surplus for all states of Rs 15.5 billion in 1979/80
dwindled to a mere Rs 2.1 billion in 1983/84. In 1984/85, for the first
time in many years, there was a current budget deficit of Rs 9.2 billion.
There has, however, been some improvement in the more recent years. A
current budget surplus of Rs 6.5 billion was realized in 1985/86, and
preliminary estimates show a surplus of Rs 2.9 billion for 1986/87. The
budgeted current surplus of Rs 9.9 billion for 1987/88 reflects substantial
additional resource mobilization by the states. However, the outcome is
likely to be less favorable, given the widespread drought in many states
and floods in the Eastern Region, and salary revisions and cost of living
adjustments.
8.       Bihar has pursued a cautious budgetary policy (Table A).  By
containing current expenditures below current revenues, the state has
achieved sizable surpluses on the current budget in recent years. This has
enabled it to more than double its capital expenIditures (including loans
and advances) over the last few years. The budgetary outcome for 1987/88
is, however, expected to be less favorable than the budget estimates
indicate because the worst floods in recent memory during the year have
seriously strained the state's finances.
9.       The budgetary position of Maharashtra deteriorated sharply in
1984/85, when the previous year's current budget surplus turned into a
large deficit (Table B). The deficit widened further in 1985/86, rising to

a level equivalent to nearly 8% of the state's current revenues. However,
imposition of substantial Ldditional taxation in 1986/87 and 1987/88,
coupled with restraint over the growth of expenditures, has helped to bring
the budgetary situation under control. The budget proposals for 1987/88
envisage a modest current budget surplus.
10.      In Rajasthan, a deficit on the current account h;; persisted from
1984/85 (Table C). An important reason for the state's budgetary
difficulties has been the prevalence of drought or near-drought conditions
in the state for the past three to four years. Drought has not only
necessitated substantial outlays for drought relief, but has also depressed
revenues. The state undertakes sizable additional resource mobilization
each year, and this is reflected in the relatively high buoyancy of its tax
revenues (see para. 12). While these efforts have helped to contain the
magnitude of deficit, they have not been sufficient to eliminate it.
11.      The budgetary situation in Uttar Pradesh improved in 1985/86 with
the achievement of a surplus (Table D). However, the surplus declined to a
negligible level in the following year, and the budgetary situation
deteriorated further in 1987/88. The budgeted current account deficit for
1987/88 is large both in relation to the state's current revenues (about
6%) and its annual additional resource mobilization efforts. With the
state exper'sencing one of the worst droughts of the century in 1987/88, an
early turnaround in the budgetary outlook seems unlikely.
Resource Mobilization
12.      The states' relatively low reliance on user charges, and limited
possibilities for influencing the growth of own capital receipts -- market
borrowing, for example, is regulated by the Central Government -- make
receipts from state-controlled taxes the most suitable indicator of
resource mobilization. The proportion of state domestic product (SDP)
taken in state (own) taxes, as alreasy indicated, varies directly with per
capita income (Table 2). Thus, the ratio of receipts from own taxes to SDP
in Maharashtra is about twice that in Bihar. Trends in these ratios
suggest that Rajasthan and Maharashtra have been more successful than the
other two states in increasing tax revenues. Buoyancy coefficients, which
indicate percentage increases in revenues associated with one percent
increase in SDP, confirm this. The buoyancy coefficients of receipts from
own taxes are estimated at 1.36 and 1.26 for Rajasthan and Maharashtra,
respectively, compared to about 1.15 for Bihar and Uttar Pradesh. The
relatively high coefficients for Rajasthan and Maharashtra suggest
substantial increases in tax rates in these states since the elasticity of
tax receipts (i.e., the automatic response of tax receipts to changes in
GDP) of Indian states is low.
Five-Year Plans and Their Implementation
13.      The states' five-year plans for 1985/86-1989/90 provide for
outlays of Rs 51 billion for Bihar, Rs 105 billion for Maharashtra, Rs 30



-142-
Annex 4.8
Page 5 of 16
billion for Rajasthan and Rs 105 billion for Uttar Pradesh 1/ (Tables E-H).
Alleviation of critical infrastructural constraints to development and
greater development of social and community services than in the past
constitute the main priorities. The largest allocations are for
agriculture, irrigation and energy; these account for 50-60% of plan
outlays. Social and community services are allocated about 17% of the
outlays in Bihar and Uttar Pradesh, and over 20% in Maharashtra and
Rajasthan. The relative shares of roads vary from 2.6% in Maharashtra to
8.8% in Uttar Pradesh.
14.      Reasonable progress is being made in the implementation of five-
year plans. During the first three years of the plan period, Maharashtra,
Rajasthan and Uttar Pradesh incurred 50-60% of the outlays proposed in
their plans. The implementation of over 70% of the plan outlays in Bihar
reflects primarily the relatively modest size of the state's plan. The
pace of progress in these states is in line with that for all states taken
together.
15.      The implementation of plan outlays on roads has kept pace with
overall plan implementation. By the end of the third year of the plan
period, Bihar and Maharashtra had incurred three-quarters of the plan
outlays on roads, and Uttar Pradesh about three-fifths of the outlays.
Rajasthan, which has spent slightly under one-half of the plan allocation,
has proposed a substantial increase in 1988/89; with the satisfactory
implementation of these higher outlays, the state will have spent nearly
three-quarters of the five-year plan outlays on roads by the penultimate
year of the plan period.
Implications of the Proposed Project for the States' Finances
16.      The project size by states takes account of the resource
constraints facing the states. Project costs would be modest in relation
to the states' total five-year plan outlays, and annual project spending
would be moderate in comparison to present levels of plan outlays on roads.
These factors would help to restrict the impact of the project on state
finances to a manageable level.
17.      Project costs as proportions of the states' five-year plans would
be under 3% in Bihar, Maharashtra and Uttar Pradesh. Annual average
project costs in relation to present levels of annual plan outlays on roads
would amount to about 25% in Bihar, 30% in Maharashtra and 20% in Uttar
Pradesh.
18.      Since Rajasthan has the lowest plan size and a relatively large
project component, the project's impact on the state's finances will be
greater. Project costs would be equivalent to about 5% of the five-year
plan outlays. Rajasthan undertakes substantial capital outlays on roads
outside the plan, so that it would not be appropriate to compare annual
project outlays with recent levels of plan outlays on roads. Thus, for
1987/88, while the likely plan outlays on roads are estimated at only Rs
I/ Plan expenditures consist of all expenditures, current as well as
capital, in respect of projects included in the plan. Five-year plans



-143-
Annex 4.8
Page 6 of 16
174 million, total capital expenditures on roads amounted to an estimated
Rs 1.4 billion. Capital outlays on roads outside the plan are mainly in
respect of centrally sponsored schemes (which are partly or wholly financed
by the Central Government) and for famine roads (which are meant to provide
drought relief). Taking account of capital expenditures umdertaken outside
the plan, annual outlays under the project would be equivalent to about 35%
of the recent levels of annual capital outlays on roads (plan and non-
plan).
19.      The project implLes the states' contributions to project costs
ranging from about Rs 125 million annually for Bihar to Rs 220 million
annually for Uttar Pradesh. To the extent that the project is accommodated
within the existing five-year plans, the additional resource requirements
will be less. The annual shares of project costs will be moderate in
relation to annual growth of capital receipts from own sources and from
untied central transfers (i.e., general purpose assistance under the Gadgil
formula, and small savings loans). With restraint over the growth of
current expenditures and reasonable additional resource mobilization
efforts, the project should not place a heavy burden on state finances.
20.      The bulk of the project outlays will be incurred from 1990/91
onwards. The recommendations of the recently-constituted Ninth Finance
Commission, and the Planning Commission on plan and non-plan transfers over
the Eighth Five Year Plan, 1990/91-1994/95 may have major implications for
atate finances during the later part of the project period.
